Crude glycerol to ethanol
fermentation in lab- and pilot-scale
Master Thesis
Christina Etler (s163095)
Main Supervisor
Associate Professor Hariklia N. Gavala,
BIOENG
Co-Supervisor
Associate Professor Ioannis V. Skiadas,
PILOT/PLANT
==PAGE:2==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Abstract
The ever-gaining attention on the production of liquid biofuels, such as biodiesel, led to
signiﬁcant production increases over the past decades. Major by-products generated, are
up to current date unused and diminish the economic viability of the biodiesel production
process [1]. One of them is glycerol, presenting a challenge, as it is obtained in raw or
crude form, imposing the necessity for expensive reﬁning or mastering the biotechnological
challenge of converting it as such to valuable products. Limitations occur based on the
presence of diﬀerent other components/impurities with a potentially inhibitory eﬀect [2].
Mixed microbial consortia show a robust and complementary metabolism, being able to
utilize multiple carbon sources and more complex substrates, including glycerol. Depending
on the metagenomic composition, diﬀerent metabolic product streams can be obtained [3].
The GRAIL EU project was focused on the development of diﬀerent applications using
crude glycerol as a substrate. Speciﬁcally, the improvement of the fermentation process is
targeted by optimizing medium composition, pH and temperature, initial substrate concen-
tration and feeding strategy [1]. Optimization of the process parameters is crucial when
working with mixed consortia, as even small variations can cause signiﬁcantly diﬀerent
outcomes.
Validation and transfer of lab-scale results into pilot-scale is crucial when aiming for an
industrially applicable technology. As previous research has already proven the viability of
cultures adapted to locally available glycerol-containing waste streams, this project focuses
on the optimization of process parameters. Lab-scale screening is evaluating the eﬀect of
pH, gas sparging rate, initial substrate concentration and feeding strategy (Part I, II, III,
IV). Finally, in Part V of the project, results are validated in pilot-scale under anaerobic
conditions. Maximum ethanol concentrations of 4.93 g/L after 48 hours and a maximum
yield of Yg/g = 0.37 are achieved under improved conditions.

==PAGE:3==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Preface
This Master Thesis Project "Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation in lab- and pilot-scale"
was carried out in continuation of the GRAIL EU project and a special course during the
13-week period of Fall 2017 Screening of operating modes and O2 supply for crude glycerol
fermentation to ethanol. The project is structured into a literature study and ﬁve diﬀerent
parts of experimental work, as outlined in Table 1.
Table 1: Parts of work within this target Master Thesis project.
Literature Study
Laboratory-Scale Screening
Part I
Screening for pH Conditions
Part II
Screening for Gas Flow Rate
Part III
Screening for Initial Substrate Concentration
Part IV
Screening for Feeding Strategy
Pilot-Scale Validation
Part V
Up-Scaling under improved Parameters
All work is carried out at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, at the Department
of Chemical Engineering.
The project was supervised by Associate Professor Hariklia
N. Gavala (main supervisor, BIOENG) and Associate Professor Ioannis V. Skiadas (co-
supervisor, PILOT PLANT).

6
Metabolite production and process metrics for fermentation at pH setpoint
5.5. Measured metabolites of Lactic Acid, Glycerol, Acetic Acid, 1,3-PDO
and Ethanol in g/L. Values have been corrected for losses through evapora-
tion; the estimated total ethanol production is given in g/L. Relevant process

1
Introduction
1.1
Introduction
Increasing petroleum prices and environmental pollution along with growing concerns on
energy security, environmental damage and sustainability have changed industrial policies
and political targets [4] [5] [6].
Biodiesel is an important example for a developing production area for high concern, show-
ing sustained growth in production volumes every year [6] [7]. Its prominent superiority
over conventional petroleum-derived diesel is not only based on the renewable nature of
its origin, but also on factors concerning health and environment (no signiﬁcant sulfur
content, low content of harmful emissions and better life-cycle of CO2 for global warming
alleviation) and its engine performance, including enhanced lubricity and a boosted oc-
tane number for complete combustion [8]. An increasing number of production plants is
observed to support and meet production demands and these national and international
energy policy targets, presenting a valuable example of a commercialized technological
platform [4][9].
Exponential growth of biodiesel production has led to concomitant increase in crude glyc-
erol generation, as it is the major by-product of the production process.
Its previous
methods for utilization and disposal included combustion, composting, anaerobic diges-
tion, animal feed production [3].
To develop future plant concepts for a long-term stable biodiesel production, it is important
to implement improved cascading of limited biomass resources, including by-products,
contributing to an eﬃcient circular economy [10].
Besides economic considerations, ubiquitous waste utilization also oﬀers the opportunity
to develop low waste, highly sustainable industries [11]. This makes glycerol a readily
accessible and competitive substrate for manufacturing commercially important products
[7].
Chemical transformation has conventionally shown several disadvantages, such as
low product speciﬁcity, the requirement of extreme temperature and pressure, as well
as the necessity of high glycerol purity, eliminating the potential to use crude glycerol
waste streams [12]. New methods include biological conversion into biofuels and green
chemicals [3]. The optimal microbial strain selection for such is of great importance. Since
genetically modiﬁed strains impose several limitations in industrial use, mixed microbial
cultures oﬀer signiﬁcant advantages for fermentative production of a variety of products [3].
They propose a "process based on [...] ecological selection principles rather than genetic or
metabolic engineering, thus combining the methodology of environmental biotechnology with
the goals of industrial biotechnology" [3]. Ethanol has a promising market and its cellulose-
Page 18 of 176
==PAGE:20==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
based production has been actively developing in the past on large scale. Fluctuations
and unstable viability are associated with the major costs devoted to pretreatment and
costs resulting from total process duration [7].
Glycerol-to-ethanol conversion is more
attractive for practical implementation compared to cellulose-based production [7]. Since
bioethanol is a clean and renewable biofuel with existing production infrastructure, it is
preferable compared to other potential products [13]. Previous research has shown that
the production from crude, while emphasizing the need for further optimization [4] [7].
1.2
Context
This thesis is carried out in continuation of the GRAIL EU project.
The project was
determined to exploit crude glycerol as a novel and valuable feedstock for biofuel and bio-
chemical production, with the aim of "preventing and valorizing bio-waste in bioreﬁneries
optimal" [1]. The target was to develop and integrate new technologies, which enable the
use of glycerol as a competitive biological feedstock under a bioreﬁnery approach. Such
integration of bio-transformation and exploration of diﬀerent fermentation conditions are
crucial to realize the project’s ultimate goal: a ﬁnal guide for the industry, consisting of
guidelines on primary glycerol transformation with selective extraction of desirable inter-
mediates and products [1].
Prior to this thesis, other research evaluated a diﬀerent source of glycerol, waste streams of
local BDF production and correspondingly two diﬀerent mixed microbial consortia, which
had previously only been tested in lab-scale. As the focus is set on locally available crude
glycerol, consortia adapted to such were included. Namely, BA-KC and MM-EF. First,
growing on BA-medium, secondly growing on minimal medium, and tested under both
microaerophilic (5% air in N2) and anaerobic conditions in pilot-scale (20L-fermenter with
75% working volume).
Mixed microbial culture BA-KC shows a maximum ethanol concentration of 1.66g/L after
48 hours and maximum yield Yg−Ethanol/g−Glycerol = 0.33 after 21 hours under anaerobic
conditions. Glycerol is ﬁrst fully consumed after 48 hours. A trade-oﬀbetween yield and
substrate conversion is the case, which is not suitable in practice. Under microaerophilic
conditions (5 % air in N2), a slightly higher maximum ethanol concentration of 1.86g/L
is obtained after 74 hours; a maximum yield of Yg−Ethanol/g−Glycerol = 0.20 shows after
24 hours. Mixed microbial culture MM-EF shows a maximum ethanol concentration of
3.96g/L and corresponding yield of Yg−EtOH/g−Glycerol = 0.33 after 24 hours under anaero-
bic conditions. Under microaerophilic conditions (5 % air in N2), a maximum concentration
of 4.12g/L and a yield of Yg−Ethanol/g−Glycerol = 0.35 are achieved after 24 hours. Glycerol
is fully consumed after 15 hours [14].
Page 19 of 176
==PAGE:21==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Based on outlined performance criteria, consortium MM-EF is chosen for this study, as
it is adapted to locally available crude glycerol and performs well in key process metrics.
Microaerophilic conditions show at the same total gas ﬂow rate compared to an anaerobic
process an improvement in yield and ﬁnal ethanol concentration. Especially MM-EF cul-
ture proofs promising in pilot-scale due to high yields on crude glycerol, high maximum
ethanol concentrations and fast substrate consumption (within 12-15 hours) [14]. For prac-
tical reasons, the down-scaled laboratory screening in this project is performed solely under
anaerobic conditions to avoid issues of oxygen mass transfer optimization.
1.3
Scope of Project
According to literature, fermentation is aﬀected by seven general factors: temperature,
pH, substrate concentration, head space conditions, supplement concentrations, inoculum
volume and agitation [15]. The scope of this projects includes the optimization of pH,
gas ﬂow rate (head space conditions), initial substrate concentration and feeding strategy.
This contributes further to paving the way to an industrially viable technology for the
fermentative use of crude glycerol. The project was carried out from 15th of January to
15th of June 2018.
Page 20 of 176
==PAGE:22==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
2
Literature Study
2.1
Biodiesel Production
Biodiesel fuel (BDF) is produced to replace depleted supplies of petroleum-based fuel
[16]. Almost all BDF production is carried out based on vegetable oil or animal fat by
transesteriﬁcation with methanol in presence of an alkaline catalyst [5] [17]. Europe has
shown to be one of the most important producers worldwide and currently accounts for
approximately 40% of global production. This includes, according to recent numbers, a
total of 427 biodiesel plants scattered across Europe, of which 286 are operational, 19
under construction, 48 planned, 49 not operational and 25 of unknown status. Due to
acquisitions, changes in ownership and bankruptcies, the total number is expected to reduce
to 347 total. The operational biodiesel plants have a total production capacity of 20,240
kt/a. Numbers from 2013 show that only 41% of the capacity is used, accounting for an
approximate production of 8,280 kt/a; the country-speciﬁc share of utilization shows to be
heterogeneous. The International Energy Agency (IEA) is expecting a European biofuel
production of approximately 20 billion litres by 2020, with moderate production increase
and potential stagnation in the post-2020 period [10]. The dynamic and rapidly expanding
market is highly dependent on the economic viability of the process [10] [18]. Biodiesel
production generates waste glycerol with an actual glycerol content of 60-80% w/w [11]
[18]. A mass production of biodiesel would entail a surplus glycerol production in a ratio of
10 % v/v (volume glycerol per volume biodiesel) [8]. With the rapid pace of development
and commercialization, it is key to ﬁnd innovative and cost eﬃcient uses for the surplus of
glycerol, to maintain economic viability of the biodiesel production industry [8].
2.2
Glycerol
Since glycerol is a by-product of the biodiesel production industry, its availability and
amount is interrelated to the development of the biodiesel industry [10]. It is a major
by-product, whose production can consume up to 4% of the respective carbon source [19].
This corresponds to approximately 10.0 wt% glycerol per kg of biodiesel (diﬀerent sources
report results in bandwidth of 9.3-10.6 wt%) [10].
Page 21 of 176
==PAGE:23==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Figure 1: Biodiesel production from vegetable oils and animal fats and its relationship with primary
oleochemical platform pathways [20]
Figure 1 details the interrelation between biodiesel production pathways and glycerol for-
mation. All reaction mechanisms of BDF and even soap production are associated with
crude glycerol generation.
The total technical potential can be derived from the biodiesel production quantity and cor-
responding share of glycerol formation [10]. This will be further evaluated when detailing
substrate availability.
This surplus of raw glycerol creates a signiﬁcant environmental problem, as it cannot be
discharged directly into the environment without further treatment [21]. Challenges include
the high costs of puriﬁcation treatment, contamination of the litospheric environment
through improper disposal and the collapsing market price of glycerol [22].
New uses
for glycerol must be proposed, proving chemical and biological conversion as both need
and opportunity [20]. Since the economic viability remains an outstanding issue in current
and future biofuel production platforms, the implementation of glycerol-based bioreﬁneries
is an option to generate value from co-products for a more eﬃcient circular economy
and sustainable footprint [10] [20]. Glycerol could be burned directly as fuel, but it is a
potentially important feedstock [9]. Since the collapsed market price of commercial glycerol
tends to limit the economic viability of crude glycerol puriﬁcation, considerable eﬀorts are
devoted to reﬁning glycerol into industrially viable products [13] [23]. Much research is
going into the limited information on use of crude glycerol, which would be the future
Page 22 of 176
==PAGE:24==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
target substrate, neglecting the necessity for any pretreatment [16].
In a glycerol-based bioreﬁnery, microbial fermentation of crude glycerol is used to pro-
duce fuels and green chemicals [18]. So far for example, successful eﬀorts for simultaneous
production of hydrogen and ethanol by pure and mixed cultures have been reported [16].
Other sources report, that glycerol-based fuel additives pose large potential for biodiesel
formulation (assist to decreasing in particles, hydrocarbons, carbon monoxide and un-
regulated aldehyde emissions, improved cold ﬂow, viscosity reducers, antiknock additives
for gasoline, octane boosters, alternative to commercial trialkylethers (MTBE, ETBE),
lower NOx emissions) [8]. Glycerol can furthermore substitute traditional carbohydrates
in some industrial fermentation processes, as it lowers treatment and raw material costs
[9]. Furthermore, it is an attractive carbon source due to a greater degree of reduction rel-
ative to sugars [24]; this advantage becomes particularly evident when synthesis of reduced
compound is considered [20]. Bioconversion is a cheap way to obtain reduced chemicals,
covering a wide realm of applications and derivatives, such as succinate, ethanol, xylitol,
propionate, hydrogen etc [9].
2.2.1
Characteristics and Properties
Glycerol (IUPAC name: propane-1,2,3-triol) is the simplest trihydric alcohol, with two
primary and a secondary hydroxyl group [8] [9]. It is commercially known under a variety of
names, including glycerin, 1,2,3-propanetriol, 1,2,3-trihydroxypropane, glyceritol or glycyl
alcohol [25] [8]. Figure 2 shows its molecular structure.
Figure 2: Molecular Structure of Glycerol [8]
The three hydroxylgroups are responsible for solubility in water and the compound’s hy-
groscopic nature [8]. Further physical and chemical properties, which are relevant in the
handling of such substance are listed in Table 5.

Glycerol is a colorless, odorless, viscous, liquid, with a speciﬁc gravity of 1.260 g/mL.
It is available for reaction with stable alcohols under most operating conditions and is
basically non-toxic to human health. Its key feature is the particular combination among
its physico-chemical properties, compatibility with other substances and easy handling [9].
As it is abundant in nature as a structural component of many lipids, this wide glycerol
occurrence allows diﬀerent kinds of microorganisms to be able to metabolize it as sole
carbon/energy source [9]. This refers to pure glycerol occurrences.
In practice, glycerol containing waste streams from BDF production are generated contain-
ing glycerol amounts of approximately 60 wt%. Further reﬁning to crude glycerol includes
basic centrifugation processes, while complete puriﬁcation remains a very cost intensive
process, which is not economically feasible in practice [9]. Table 3 shows the approximate
glycerol concentration and the diﬀerent classiﬁcation of the glycerol-containing stream.
Table 3: Diﬀerentiation of diﬀerent types of glycerol waste streams and their corresponding content of
glycerol in wt% [9].
Raw Glycerol
60 wt%
Crude Glycerol
88 wt%
Pure Glycerol
98 wt%
Page 24 of 176
==PAGE:26==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
The microbial utilization of puriﬁed glycerol would be more eﬃcient, but imposes signiﬁ-
cantly higher processing costs. As a waste stream, it is a low cost starting material, but
tends to be highly oxygenated, impure, aqueous and dilute. The complex waste material
contains high amounts of contaminants, e.g. soaps, FFAs, salts, ashes and methanol, which
can cause problematic interference and inhibitory patterns with microbial metabolism. If
derived from butchery waste based BDF production, it is considered to have even more
impurities (high amount of sulfur and FFAs, very low pH) [3]. The speciﬁc composition
is very much dependent on the respective BDF production feedstock used and catalyst
employed in the transesteriﬁcation process [26]. High levels of a variety of contaminants
are alwaysvisible, in particular methanol, which is a cytotoxic substance [27].
2.2.2
Availability
Glycerol represents an ubiquitous waste material with the opportunity for low waste, highly
sustainable industries [11]. The practical feasibility of glycerol-based plant concepts cor-
respondingly depends on feedstock availability and techno-economic performance, both of
its conversion process, but also of the biodiesel production it is originating from [10]. The
oleochemical industry generates waste streams, containing 55-90% glycerol [20]. The avail-
ability of glycerol is thereby highly interrelated to the development of biodiesel production
and it is predicted, that crude glycerol availability will increase alongside the global growth
in biodiesel production [22].
Currently, 203 fully operational biodiesel production plants in Europe operate with a total
capacity of 20,240 kt/y capacity, out of which 41% is currently used. Using the reported 10
wt% share of glycerol production as a co-product, the technical and theoretical potential
of total annual availability can be calculated as outlined in Equations 1 2 and 3 [10].
POT = PROD[kt/y] · GLY [%]
(1)
POTtheoretical = 2, 024[kg/y]
(2)
POTtechnical = 828[kt/y]
(3)
Two thirds of the calculated glycerol availability potential is associated with large-scale
biodiesel plants, eacch producing an amount greater than 10 kt/y of glycerol. This inter-
relation is reﬂected in the glycerol availability map shown in Figure 3.
Page 25 of 176
==PAGE:27==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Figure 3: Theoretical and technical glycerol potentials in kt/a from operational biodiesel plants in Europe
[10].
An availability concentration can be observed in a belt from northern France to north-
eastern parts of Germany, northern Italy, eastern Greece and the Iberian Peninsula. Even
though forecasts of the biodiesel market development imply a doubling of the current
amount of glycerol by 2020 (prospected to still be produced in large scale until at least
2030), only certain regions in Europe are suitable for large-scale future plant concepts of
glycerol conversion, considering logistical aspects. Large transportation distances impose
an increasing risk for costs and eco-eﬃciency, leaving network operation as a potential
alternative [10]. Glycerol is thereby a highly available substrate, but for an economically
feasible, industrial bioconversion concepts, signiﬁcant improvements still need to be made
within conversion technologies.
2.3
Strain Selection and the Potential of Mixed Microbial Cultures
2.3.1
Glycerol Degradation and Reaction Mechanism
Glycerol is dissimilated by distinct metabolic pathways in microorganisms. Many microor-
ganisms can metabolize glycerol in presence of external electron acceptors (respiratory
metabolism), but only a few can do so fermentatively [9].
Page 26 of 176
==PAGE:28==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Fermentative metabolism is reported for several species of the Enterobacteriaceae family,
including Escherichia coli, Klebsiella pneumoniae and other species from the genera of
Clostridium, Lactobacillus, Bacillus, Propionibacterium and Anaerobiospirillum [28]. Spe-
ciﬁc reaction pathways depend on the respective strain, but share common characteristics.
The highly reduced nature of carbon atoms in glycerol requires microorganisms that can
metabolize it in the absence of external electron acceptors. The degree of reduction per
carbon in glycerol of κ=4.67 is signiﬁcantly higher compared to κ=4 per carbon atoms in
e.g. glucose and xylose. To maintain the overall redox poise within the cell, two parallel
pathways, a reductive and an oxidative one take place. The reductive pathway acquires
the production of a product that is more reduced than glycerol, forming a "sink" for the
excess of reducing equivalents generated in cell mass formation [28].
Figure 4: Reductive glycerol assimilation for production of a more reduced compound [28].
As shown in Figure 4, several options are available for the reductive pathway, depending
on the respective microorganism; 1,3-PDO-, 1,2-PDO and propanediol-dependent. Figure
?? shows the conversion in more detail, as pathways diﬀer slightly, also with regards to
the relation of reductive to oxidative pathway.
Page 27 of 176
==PAGE:29==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
(a) Reductive pathway for 1,3-PDO dependent
processes.
(b) Reductive pathway for 1,2-PDO dependent
processes.
Figure 5: Reductive glycerol assimilation for production of a more reduced compound [28]. Pathway (a)
is 1,3-PDO dependent; Pathway (b) for 1,2-PDO dependent processes.
The 1,3-PDO dependent pathway (Figure 5a) involves the conversion of glycerol to 3-HPA
through a coenzyme B12-dependent glycerol dehydratase and then further conversion to
1,3-PDO by a NADH-dependent 1,3-PDO dehydrogenase [28].
The 1,2-PDO dependent pathway diﬀers slightly, as the conversion to DHA and DHAP is
common for both oxidative and the reductive pathway. In case of a separate oxidative con-
version, glycerol is dehydrogenated to DHA through a NAD-linked glycerol dehydrogenase
and then phosphoylated to DHAP by a PEP- or ATP dependent DHA kinase (function
depends on aerobic or anaerobic conditions). DHAP then enters glycolysis towards the
production of pyruvate, from which diﬀerent NAD/NADH-linked pathways lead to the
production of other essential intermediates and fermentation products. These include, de-
pending on the microorganism, succinic acid, formic acid, ethanol, acetic and lactic acid,
butyric acid and butanol [21] [27] [28].
Figure 6 provides an overview on the biochemical path of the oxidative fermentation path-
way and the spectrum of potential products originating from the intermediate pyruvate.
Page 28 of 176
==PAGE:30==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Figure 6: Oxidative pathway glycerol assimilation for production of a spectrum of fermentation products
[29].
It is assumed that other mechanisms and pathways mediating the dissimilation of glycerol
are existing based on experimental observations in previous studies, but such have not
been explored yet [28]. The interest in natural converters is high, as mentioned above,
but cannot be easily practically realized, as glycerol is not available in its pure, but rather
raw or crude form. Impurities impose major challenges, to obtain microbes tolerant to
inhibitors such as salts and organic solvents, which are present in raw and crude glycerol
waste streams [18].
2.3.2
Novel Strains and Metabolic Engineering
Previous research has focused on using microbial screening for the discovery of novel strains
and there have been several attempts to explore microbial substrate assimilation using
reconstructed microbial systems via microbial screening or metabolic pathway engineering
[18]. A huge interest in natural producers of a variety of products is present, but such
are generally characterized by low productivity, especially when growing on crude or raw
glycerol streams [7].
Page 29 of 176
==PAGE:31==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Metabolic engineering functions as a method to broaden the carbon substrate spectrum
usable to speciﬁc microorganisms [27]. It enables deletion and over-expression of speciﬁc
pathways, altering the resulting product spectrum. As a result from various metabolic en-
gineering eﬀorts, fuels such as ethanol, hydrogen and biogas as well as chemicals including
1,3-PDO, organic acids and other products were found to be produced by microbial fer-
mentation of glycerol from various mutant strains [18]. Microorganisms of interest included
pathway engineered E. coli strains for ethanol production, Clostridium pasteurianum mu-
tant for butanol production, engineered Klebsiella planticola for ethanol and formate pro-
duction or the co-production of ethanol and hydrogen by engineered Enterobacter aerogenes
mutants [18] [20].
At an industrial scale of application, the use of genetically modiﬁed organisms is associated
with signiﬁcant limitations, predominantly environmental issues [7]. The diverse problems
include the pathogenic level, strict anaerobic conditions, complex cultivation media and
the requirement of a sterile environment, due to the high process sensitivity to contami-
nation [30] [9]. Such conditions and issues are hard to meet, while keeping the production
competitive and economically feasible.
2.3.3
Mixed Microbial Consortia (MMCs)
When working towards meeting the mentioned issues, overcoming inhibition problems
and enhancing substrate degradation eﬃciency, mixed microbial consortia (MMC) oﬀer
a promising alternative [3]. They give the opportunity of working under non-sterile condi-
tions, while neglecting any reﬁning treatment of the substrate prior to fermentation, which
lowers production costs signiﬁcantly. The higher microbial diversity with complementary
metabolisms enables them to degrade more complex substrates [13]. That makes them
particularly advantageous compared to pure cultures when dealing with crude glycerol,
which has variable impurities [13].
Diﬀerent dominant metabolic products are obtained, depending on the respective selection
strategy and reaction conditions, the main ones including 1,3-PDO, ethanol and organic
acids. The stability of the consortium is thereby reﬂected in the distribution of the main
metabolites; changes in the microbial composition can be detailed by next generation
sequencing [3].
When working with mixed cultures, it is important to diminish the production of competing
metabolites by adjustment of fermentation conditions, including optimized nutritional and
environmental parameters such as pH, temperature, aeration and substrate concentration
[23] [16]. Optimization of such is carried out in this case by conventional one-factor-at-a-
time technique. This has the advantage of directly identifying the eﬀect of changes in one
Page 30 of 176
==PAGE:32==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
factor, but does not give the possibility to illustrate interactive eﬀects among the tested
variables. Within appropriate ranges, changes in fermentation parameters, such as pH and
temperature, inﬂuence the growth of diﬀerent species, as well as the activity of involved
enzymes, including hydrogenases and alcohol dehydrogenases, which are among others the
enzymes responsible for hydrogen and ethanol production [16].
2.4
Choice of Product: Ethanol
Diﬀerent glycerol utilization pathways cover a wide range of potential applications, ranging
from food supplements (vitamin B12, β -carotene, DHA and trehalose) over green chemicals
(1,3-PDO, PHAs and other polymers) to biofuels, including ethanol, hydrogen, butanol and
fatty acid glycerol formal ester (FAGE) [10] [26]. The industrial realization of respective
production lines is among other factors, restricted by feedstock demand and corresponding
conversion eﬃciency, diminishing the viability of a variety of potential plant concepts [10].
The choice of product is further inﬂuenced by its market demand and conversion eﬃciency.
The culture of choice and reaction conditions, inﬂuence the spectrum of metabolic products.
In particular focus of past research has been 1,3-propanediol (1,3-PDO); the choice of
product in this study is ethanol.
1,3-PDO is a renewable platform chemical, serving a variety of applications; such include
building blocks in polymer production, cosmetic and pharmaceutical industry, feedstock
for adhesives and food applications [10]. Its reductive metabolic pathway is used by mi-
croorganisms to maintain a redox balance within the cell. Using this pathway, research has
yielded a ﬁnal product concentration of 93.7 g/L and a productivity of 3.3 g/L/h from pure
glycerol and 76.2 g/L and 2.3 g/L/h from raw glycerol, respectively [11]. Fermentation of
glycerol to 1,3-PDO has been studied using Klebsiella pneumoniae, Citrobacter freundii,
Clostridium butyricum and Enterobacter agglomerans [11]. Main bottlenecks include its
diﬃcult extraction from water, due to the high water solubility, requiring further targeted
research into newer solvent technologies, e.g. imidazolium and phosphonium ionic liquids
[11].
Ethanol represents a promising product from glycerol fermentation, proven advantageous
during previous research studies.
Applications of ethanol cover a variety of ﬁelds but are mainly found as raw material,
potential as a supplement to gasoline (blend in gasoline engines 5-85%) and feedstock in
production of oxygenated fuels, e.g. by replacing methanol [10] [4] [5]. In general, the
fermentative production of ethanol has been performed successfully for several years [10].
The cost of production is mainly governed by feedstock and operational cost. The use of
crude glycerol instead of traditional feedstock could reduce the total production costs by
Page 31 of 176
==PAGE:33==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
about 40% by eliminating the need for pre-treatment of substrate, expensive downstream
processing and a much lower raw material cost, since crude glycerol is available as a waste
material [13]. Upon working with mixed microbialc onsortia, few members of Citrobacter,
Klebsiella, Enterobacter and Escherichia are reported to produce ethanol as major products
of glycerol conversion. The fermentation passes through pyruvate, which generates twice
the amount of reducing equivalents compared to sugar-based fermentation, representing
another advantage for switching substrate.
The stoichiometry of its production reaction based on crude glycerol is shown in Equation
4 and 5.
C3H8O3 →C2H5OH + H2 + CO2
(4)
C3H8O3 →C2H5OH + HCOOH
(5)
As visible above, this biological co-fermentation of glycerol to ethanol and hydrogen is
particularly attractive, since hydrogen is generated in signiﬁcant amounts, especially under
microaerobic conditions through the enzymes pyruvate formate lyase and formate hydrogen
lyase. Hydrogen is expected to be one of the future clean energy sources and could be
applied in hydrogen-oxygen fuel cells [22] [10]. Combined production process provides a
higher total energy yield [16]; both products yield a theoretical maximum of 1 mole/mole
[4]. In this study, hydrogen production is neither measured or evaluated on; it remains for
future research.
One of the main challenges of this production remains the low productivity and low ﬁnal
concentration of ethanol. Further challenges are presented by the optimization of fermen-
tation conditions, which is crucial when working with mixed microbial consortia to obtain
the target dominant product and minimize by-product formation. Concepts of pH and
temperature optimization as well as the application of a micro-aerobic environment aim at
reducing the accumulation of by-products in this glycerol-to-ethanol fermentation.
Besides this, ethanol and hydrogen production present some advantages in comparison with
obtaining other potential products, as mentioned above. The conversion of crude glycerol
into biofuels such as biohydrogen and bioethanol shows to be more attractive compared
with pyrolysis, electrolytic and reforming processes [4]. Furthermore, simultaneous produc-
tion of two products is yielding a better total energy eﬃciency of the process. Production
is exhibited in separate phases, favoring cost eﬃcient downstream processing. [4].
Page 32 of 176
==PAGE:34==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
2.5
Ethanol loss by evaporation
Ethanol, as the target product of this fermentation optimization project, is a very volatile
compound. Continuous gas stripping, as carried out, facilitates the stripping of such fer-
mentation gases. As the product is not trapped and collected, losses through evaporation
have to be estimated to quantify the total ethanol production over time and accurately
calculate eﬀective process parameters. For this purpose, loss estimation experiments are
carried out. All setups throughout this project are evaluated under the premise of success-
fully capturing the total ethanol produced.
Currently applied technologies for this purpose include the utilization of membrane biore-
actors, pervaporation system, which have successfully been applied to continuous alcohol
fermentation processes. It allows for simultaneous extraction and concentration of ethanol
[31]. A combination of membranes is recommended, using membranes selectively for per-
meation of ethanol at the front end to remove ethanol from the fermentation broth and
water-permeable membranes at the back end, to further concentration the ethanol [32].
Ethanol stripping columns and a combination of cold traps using e.g. liquid nitrogen are
other options to replace conventional, energy-intensive distillation processes [33].
2.5.1
Loss Estimation Experiments
Ethanol loss estimation experiments are carried out under accurate fermentation condi-
tions, including temperature, gas ﬂow rate, active volume and media composition. Ethanol
is added to a known concentration at the start and then monitored over time. The loss
estimated follows ﬁrst order kinetics; a loss rate constant k, depending on the measured
ethanol concentration in the broth can be calculated. Based on k and the respective mea-
sured ethanol concentration in the fermentation broth, loss rate and cumulative ethanol
loss can be calculated. The total ethanol production can then be estimated for each setup.
Detailed data on all calculations for each volume and ﬂow rate can be found in Appendix
D.
2.5.2
Evaporation modelling
Alternatively, diﬀerent approaches exist to mathematically model the loss of evaporation.
These theoretical calculations can consider many more factors, such as geometry and type
of condenser, other compounds present during the fermentation, etc. It is particularly
useful to get an order-of-magnitude estimation before the start of an experiment. Due to
a lack of technical data, this could not be applied in this study; it has been attempted in
previous studies [34] [35].
Page 33 of 176
==PAGE:35==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
To contain the ethanol losses, a condensing system was operated for oﬀ-gas cooling in such
way, that almost total ethanol retention was ensured. Small losses and deviations have
been quantiﬁed through loss estimation experiments, as outlined in Appendix D and G.
Page 34 of 176
==PAGE:36==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
3
Materials and Methods
3.1
Process Overview
Figure 7 provides a brief overview on the experimental procedure followed in this project.
Screening steps are carried out in lab-scale on pH, gas ﬂow rate and initial substrate
concentration; batch and fed-batch operation are compared and the ﬁnal step includes the
validation and up-scaling of screening results into pilot-scale.
Figure 7: Outline of the diﬀerent steps within the experimental procedure.
Table 4 gives an overview on the experimental screening procedure followed during this
project. Laboratory Screening for improved conditions is carried out for pH, gas ﬂow rate
and initial substrate concentration. For pH, values of 5.5, 6.0, 6.5 and 7.0 are tested, each
as a continuous setpoint and minimum value. Gas sparging rates of 0.0 L-N2/min, 0.15
L-N2/min, 0.5 L-N2/min, 1.0 L-N2/min and 1.5 L-N2/min are considered. Initial glycerol
concentrations of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L are evaluated.

the entire process.

3.2
Substrate
All fermentation processes of this study were based on crude glycerol as substrate. It is
obtained from butchery waste-based biodiesel production from the facilities of Daka Eco-
Motion Biodiesel, Denmark. The detailed composition of such impure substrate depends
on the basis of the biodiesel production process it was obtained from; substrate charac-
terization and zero-samples are therefore inevitable for an accurate evaluation. The main
properties of this studies’ crude glycerol are reported in Table 5 [3].
Table 5: Characteristic composition of crude glycerol obtained from Daka EcoMotion Biodiesel, Denmark
[3].
Component
Approximate Value
Raw Glycerine
75%
Fat
10%
Methanol
<1%
Sulphur
1-2%
Moisture
10%
Ash
5%
Density
1.2-1.3 kg/L
pH
1.5
(adjusted to pH 7 for fermentation purposes)
It shows a total glycerol concentration of approximately 75%, along with several impurities,
including mainly methanol, sulphur and free fatty acids. Approximate pH when obtained
is measured between 1.0 and 1.5, due to sulphuric acid used during the respective biodiesel
production process. For fermentation purposes, the pH is adjusted to approximately pH
6.5-7.0 using 4M KOH. To improve substrate homogeneity and ﬂow properties, the pH
adjusted glycerol is centrifuged at 5000 rpm for 15 min, to remove a majority of the sludge
sediment.
3.3
Media Composition
Fermentation processes are all based on minimal medium (MM). It contains per liter of
deionized water: 2.59 g K2HPO4, 1.3 g KH2PO4, 2.0 g (NH4)2SO4, 0.2 g MgSO4 ·7H2O,
0.02 g CaCl2 ·2H2O and 0.005 g FeSO4 ·7H2O. For inoculum activation and pre-cultivation
setups, additional 0.02 L tryptone solution (prepared the same day, concentration 100 g/L
tryptone SIGMA T7293 or similar) were added per liter of medium as growth substrate.
Page 36 of 176
==PAGE:38==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
3.4
Inoculum
The inoculum is a mixed microbial consortium based on activated sludge. It has been pre-
viously cultivated on minimal medium; transfer strategy was based on end-of-fermentation,
including transfers every 72 hours. It will further on be called MM-EF (MM = Minimal
Medium; EF = End-of-Fermentation) [3] [2]. It has previously been optimized towards the
degradation of complex substrates, speciﬁcally crude glycerol from butchery waste-based
BDF production, also under non-sterile conditions [3].
For practical reasons, the down-scaled laboratory screening in this project is performed
solely under anaerobic conditions to avoid issues of oxygen mass transfer optimization.
MM-EF is characterized by a dominance of glycerol consuming species, including Clostrid-
ium, Klebsiella and Escherichia strains. Repeated transfers in small batches and fed-batch
conditions have been applied in previous studies, comparing the use of diﬀerent inocula,
growth media, kinetic control and microaeration. Transfers have been diﬀerentiated by
kinetic control (KC), where the transfer is made during the late exponential growth phase
and end-of-fermentation (EF), where the transfer is made after 72 hours, when no more
fermentation gasses are produced. The selected culture was cultivated on minimal medium
(MM) and transferred at end-of-fermentation.
Metagenomic classiﬁcation revealed the approximate microbial composition, mainly includ-
ing Clostridium, Klebsiella and Escherichia species. Other species present include among
others Lactobacillus, Enterobacter, Slackia, Serratia and Tolumonas [3].
In previous studies, a sequence of dominance shifts was observed in relation to reaction
conditions. Dominance shifts in metagenomic classiﬁcation were additionally correlated
with shifts in dominant metabolic products over repeated transfers [3].
Ethanol peaks
were associated with a dominance of Escherichia, while an increase of Klebsiella dominance
shifts the main metabolic product to 1,3-PDO and a butyric acid increase can be related
to the dominance of Clostridium [3].
The consortia have previously shown substrate degradation eﬃciency of 90-95%. They
were obtained from activated sludge collected from a wastewater treatment plant of Daka
Biodiesel, Denmark, whee it can be assumed to be already enriched with microbes able to
utilize glycerol and lipid substances as carbon source [3].
Each setup is inoculated with 10% v/v of inoculum to avoid a noticable lag phase.
Page 37 of 176
==PAGE:39==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
3.5
Activation and Pre-Cultivation
Pre-activated consortia MM-EF were obtained from storage at -18°C. For cultivation,
these were refrigerated at 8°C for 2 hours and left an additional hour at room temperature
for thawing. Preparation of pre-cultures in suﬃcient amounts was done in a single-step
procedure for lab-scale processes and a two-step pre-cultivation process for pilot-scale fer-
mentation. Several small 50 mL pre-cultures are pre-pared in 120 mL serum vials, by
adding 1 g crude glycerol, 25 mL of 2x concentrated minimal growth medium (MM), 20
mL deionized water (DW), 130 µL 4M KOH, 1 mL of concentrated tryptone solution and
5 mL of vortexed inoculum. Vials are ﬂushed with N2 for 3 minutes; the vials are closed
and incubated at 37°C and 130 rpm for 21 hours. After incubation, produced total gas
volume is measured (using a 20 mL piston closed syringe by repeated degassing until no
piston movement), OD600 is measured by photospectroscopy; a sample for HPLC analysis
is taken and prepared. Good pre-activation should reach around 80-100 mL gas and an op-
tical density of approximately OD600 = 2 (calculated from measurement under appropriate
dilution). For pilot-scale, a second step of pre-cultivation is carried out to provide suﬃcient
amount of culture for scaled up fermentation (the amount of inoculum should account for
10% of the total active fermentation volume to avoid a noticeable lag phase). Several 500
mL pre-cultures are prepared in 1 L serum bottles on the same basis, adding proportional
amounts as listed before. 50 mL pre-cultures are used as inoculum; incubation proceeds
under the same conditions as before and the same measurements are taken. Resulting, 1.5
L of inoculum are available for inoculation at pilot-scale.
3.6
Analytical Methods
Samples of 2 mL were taken before transfer of each pre-culture and during batch fermenta-
tion at 0, 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12, 24 and 48 hours of fermentation. The loss of volume is considered
negligible compared to the total volume and is not considered when evaluating results. All
samples were analyzed with regards to optical density OD600 through photospectroscopy,
which is a turbidity measurement. Cell biomass was obtained by correlating the optical
density with the dry cell mass according to the calibration data shown in Appendix F.
1OD600 = 1.31g −dm/L
(6)
To obtain this, a 15mL sample was collected and optical density measured at a wavelength
of 600 nm. a Gelman Supor-450 or Sartorius cellulose nitrate 0.45 µ m ﬁlter is dried on
a glass dish in the microwave oven at 180 W for 10 min. the ﬁlter is transferred to a
desiccator and cooled down for 10-15 min. The ﬁlter is weighed on an analytical balance.
The sample cell suspension is ﬁltered through the ﬁlter using a vacuum ﬁltration pump.
Page 38 of 176
==PAGE:40==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
The cells are washed with DW three times, using the same volume as the ﬁltrated each
time. The ﬁlter is transferred onto the glass dish and let dry in the microwave oven for
15 min at 180 W. The ﬁlters are transferred to the dessicator and let cool for 10-15min
and weighed afterwards on an analytical balance. the weight of the dried ﬁlter paper is
substracted from the measurement to obtain the dry cell biomass weight.
For analysis of ethanol, glycerol and organic acid production, HPLC samples were prepared.
This was done by centrifugation of the sample at 10000 rpm for 10 min, ﬁltration and
adding 350 µL of sample with 350 µL of eluent (12 mM H2SO4) into each HPLC vial.
Each sample was analyzed for lactic acid, glycerol, acetic acid, propionic acid, 1,3 PDO,
iso-butyric acid, butyric acid, ethanol, valeric acid, 1-butanol and caproic acid. Obtained
values were normalized with regards to standards and dilution factors. The HPLC used
was a Shimadzu HPLC, running on a BioRAD aminex HPX-87H column with 12 mM
H2SO4 eluent at a ﬂow rate of 0.6 mL/min and an operating pressure of approximately 45
bars..
Since ethanol is a very volatile fermentation product and especially in case of continuous
gas sparging, the stripping of fermentation gases is pronounced, ethanol loss through evap-
oration occurs. Results are corrected for these losses based on results from loss estimation
experiments. Details of such can be found in Appendix D. The given values account for
the total ethanol production, including both the measured concentration in the broth and
the estimated loss.
All processes are evaluated under the premise that all ethanol can be captured externally.
This was not practically carried out in this project.
3.7
Laboratory-Scale Screening
Laboratory screening is carried out in 1L lab-scale bench-top reactors from the Infors
Multifors series. Figure 8 provides a schematic and realistic view on the Infors Multifors
bench-top fermentation system.
Page 39 of 176
==PAGE:41==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
(a) Schematic view on the Infors Multifors
bench-top reactors.
(b) View on the Infors Multifors bench-top re-
actors
Figure 8: Schematic and realistic view on the Infors Multifors bench-top fermentation system.
Salts for 1L minimal medium are weighed dry into the fermenter, crude glycerol is added,
corresponding to the targeted initial glycerol concentration. The fermenter is ﬁlled up to a
total volume of 900 mL using deionized water under continuous stirring at 350 rpm. The
reactor is heated through a heating jacket to an operating temperature of 37°C. The oﬀ-gas
is cooled through an internal condensing system to a temperature of 8°C to minimize loss
of volatile metabolites. Any losses are quantiﬁed based on loss estimation experiments
(Details can be found in Appendix D). Before inoculation, the system is ﬂushed with 2 L-
N2/min for 15 min to ensure anaerobic conditions; afterwards the gas ﬂow rate is adjusted
to the respective value. pH and redox probes are calibrated; pH setpoint or minimum is set
Page 40 of 176
==PAGE:42==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
and controlled using either only 2M KOH or both 2M KOH and 2M H2SO4. The system is
inoculated with 100 mL anaerobically pre-activated culture of mixed microbial consortium
MM-EF, yielding a ﬁnal active volume of 1 L and a head space volume of approximately
0.5 L. The volume of inoculum amounts for 10% of the total fermentation volume, which
has shown to prevent a noticeable lag time in previous studies [12].
The system and its parameters are controlled using the internal Infors Multifors system.
Temperature is controlled via a heating jacket; pH control is based on a PID control system.
Parameters are monitored by Iris 6.0 software at an interval of 2 minutes.
Screening for improved process parameters is carried out under a waterfall approach, es
described in the following.
3.7.1
pH Screening
For screening of diﬀerent pH conditions during fermentation, pH values of pH 5.5, 6.0, 6.5
and 7.0 (chosen based on literature review) are tested in two diﬀerent conﬁgurations. Each
pH value is tested as a continuous setpoint, controlled by 2M KOH and 2M H2SO4, as well
as a minimum, controlled solely by 2M KOH. The process is run anaerobically with an
initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L (not optimized, chosen based on previous research
[36] [14]) and nitrogen sparging at a ﬂow rate of 1.5 L/min on an active fermentation volume
of 1L.
3.7.2
Gas Flow Rate Screening
Best performing pH is adapted based on the previous screening and put to the secondary
screening step; the chosen initial substrate concentration is kept the same. Diﬀerent gas
ﬂow rates are tested for their eﬀect on the metabolite production. As the processes on lab-
scale are run anaerobic, only nitrogen gas is sparged. Tested ﬂow rates include no sparging
(0.0 L/min), 0.15 L/min, 0.5 L/min, 1.0 L/min and 1.5 L/min at an active volume of 1L
fermentation broth. Flow rates are tested based on the liquid volume and its number of
exchanges per minute.
3.7.3
Initial Substrate Concentration Screening
Optimized pH setpoint and gas sparging rate are adapted for the tertiary step of screening.
Diﬀerent initial glycerol concentrations are tested for their inﬂuence on metabolic outcome
and process performance. Tested concentrations include 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35
Page 41 of 176
==PAGE:43==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
g/L. The amount of crude glycerol to be added respectively was calculated based on a
glycerol content of 75% in the crude feed stream.
3.7.4
Feeding Strategy
Final parameter to investigate is the feeding strategy of this fermentation. Adapting the
previously optimized parameters, the process is run in fed-batch operation. In contrast
to a batch process, where the full substrate is added in the beginning, during fed-batch
operation, substrate is added in cycles up to a certain concentration, aiming to increase
the total product concentration and overall yield of the process.
The process is run at a gas sparging rate of 0.0 L-N2/min to aim for an increased ethanol
concentration in the broth, since no signiﬁcant losses are estimated. Based on the glycerol
degradation rate shown in batch, the process is fed every 12 hours to a glycerol concen-
tration as screened to be optimal. The same volume of glycerol is also added of minimal
medium, to ensure suﬃcient availability of nutrients. The process is run for 99 hours. To
aim for a maximum yield, the process is also run at a gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min
to aim for maximum production of ethanol. Based on the glycerol degradation shown in
batch, the process is fed every 9 hours to a glycerol concentration as screened for, and the
same volume of minimal medium is added to ensure suﬃcient availability of nutrients. The
process is run for 126 hours. Samples are taken every 2 hours within the ﬁrst 12 hours and
afterwards to the best possibilities at the beginning and end of each feeding cycle.
3.8
Pilot-Scale Validation
The improved parameters are scaled up to pilot-scale, comparing to a 20 L glass-fermenter
in this case.
Figure 9 shows a view on the setup, including the fermenter and the cooling unit (heating
unit cannot be seen).
Page 42 of 176
==PAGE:44==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
Figure 9: View of the pilot fermentation system, including the external cooling unit.
The system includes the fermenter unit itself, nitrogen and air supply lines and in-build gas
ﬂow meters, as well as an external cooling and heating unit, connecting to the tab water.
The system is controlled using LabVIEW software, enabling continuous data logging. A
complete updated fermenter manual, including piping and instrumentation diagram, start-
up procedures, operation and cleaning procedures is attached in Appendix E.
Salts for 15L medium are weighed dry into the fermenter, crude glycerol, corresponding
to an initial substrate concentration as optimized in lab-scale is added as fermentation
substrate. 13.2 L of deionized water are added under continuous stirring at 350 rpm. The
reactor is heated to a stable operating temperature of 37°C by an external heating unit;
the oﬀ-gas is cooled through an external cooling unit to 4°C to avoid excessive substrate
loss of volatile products. Evaporative losses are estimated through a set of loss estimation
experiments Appendix D. The given amounts show the total ethanol production, including
both the measured concentration and estimated losses. pH and O2 electrode are connected
and calibrated. The system is ﬂushed with 2 L-N2/min for 30 min to ensure anaerobic
conditions; afterwards the gas ﬂow is adjusted to respective nitrogen ﬂow rate. Selection
is done based on lab-scale screening of diﬀerent ﬂow rates, which are then scaled up based
on the active liquid volume in the fermenter and its exchanges per minute. This does not
consider the head space volume or the surface-to-volume ratio within the fermenter. The
system is inoculated with 1.5 L pre-cultures of mixed microbial consortium MM-EF to an
OD of approximately 0.1. The total fermentation volume is adjusted to 15L using deionized
water; pH is adjusted to setpoint and further controlled automatically by addition of 2M
KOH and 2M H2SO4 based on a PID control system.

==PAGE:45==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
4
Results and Discussion
4.1
pH Screening
Figure 10 shows the pH screening, as the ﬁrst step of the experimental procedure of this
project.
Figure 10: Status of the experimental Process.
During lab-scale screening of pH conditions, pH 5.5, 6.0, 6.5 and 7.0 were selected as test
values based on literature research and previous experiments. Each pH value was tested
as a setpoint, controlled by 2M KOH and 2M H2SO4, and as a minimum, controlled only
by 2M KOH. Results are compared and discussed with regards to metabolite produc-
tion and relevant process metrics, including degradation rate, productivity and yield of
ethanol production. Measured ethanol concentrations are corrected by estimated losses
through evaporation, in accordance with Appendix D; given values account for the total
ethanol production. Cell concentration is monitored by measurements of optical density
and converted according to the calibration setup in Appendix F. Detailed raw data on all
metabolite measurements and fermentation conditions can be found in Appendix C and
Appendix F.
4.1.1
pH 5.5
pH Setpoint 5.5
pH 5.5 is ﬁrst evaluated as a continuous setpoint, controlled by 2M KOH and 2M H2SO4.
pH conditions and concentrations of cell biomass, glycerol, ethanol and 1,3-PDO are dis-
played in Figure 11; detailed metabolites over time and relevant process metrics are shown
in Table 6. Measured ethanol concentrations are corrected for losses through evaporation;
values given account for the estimated total ethanol production in g/L.

The pH setpoint of 5.5 is stable throughout the fermentation. Maximum cell density is
observed after 48 hours with 3.14 g-dm/L. Substrate concentration and main metabolites,
including ethanol and 1,3-PDO are showing in Figure 11; detailed metabolites and key
process metrics are shown in Table 6.
Glycerol is fully consumed within 12-15 hours;
maximum ethanol production is estimated to 3.50 g/L after 24 hours fermentation time.
Notable by-products include 1,3-PDO, reaching a maximum concentration of 4.03 g/L after
48 hours; minor amount of acetic acid and other organic acids are present.
Degradation is most eﬃcient between 6 and 12 hours fermentation time, reaching a max-
Page 45 of 176
==PAGE:47==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
imum of 1.70 g/L/h; productivity is increasing until 12 hours fermentation time, with a
maximum of 0.50 g/L/h. Yields in g/g after full glycerol consumption reach a maximum
of 0.32 g/g after 12 hours. As the maximum production based on stoichiometry is 1 mole
ethanol per mole glycerol, the yield in mole/mole indicates also how much of the theoretical
yield is achieved; 0.65 mole/mole after 12 hours are achieved.

of 3.65 g-dm/L is monitored after 48 hours. Glycerol concentration and main metabolites
ethanol and 1,3-PDO are shown over time in Figure 12; detailed metabolite production
and key process metrics given in Table 7.

Glycerol degradation is completed within 12 hours; ethanol production reaches a maximum
of 3.18 g/L within 12 hours. This value results from the measured ethanol concentration
in the fermentation broth and the estimated losses by evaporation. Notable by-products

after 12 hours.

ﬁrst 8 hours of fermentation and signiﬁcantly decreasing afterwards, since glycerol is fully
consumed after 10 hours. Productivity increases simultaneously to 0.72 g/L/h (8 hours
fermentation time) and decreases afterwards with decreased substrate availability. Yields
in gram ethanol per gram glycerol reach maximum of 0.34 after 12 hours and decrease
to 0.29 after 48 hours. Yields in mole per mole correspondingly range from 0.69 after 12
hours to 0.44 after 48 hours, respectively.

g-dm/L is monitored after 24 hours. Figure 15 gives an overview on these parameters
together with substrate and key metabolite concentrations; other metabolite production
and process metrics are detailed in Table 10. Ethanol concentrations have been corrected
for evaporative losses; the values given account for the total ethanol production, including
the measured concentration and estimated losses.

Glycerol is fully degraded after 8-10 hours, with a maximum degradation rate of 2.99 g/L/h

is the main by-product throughout the process, reaching maximum concentrations of 4.11
g/L after 12 hours; minor amounts of lactic and acetic acid are present.
4.1.5
Discussion of pH screening
Diﬀerent pH conditions inﬂuence the activity of diﬀerent enzymes involved in the respective
conversion process, which each have their respective optimum. Changes in pH value alter
the enzymatic activity and correspondingly favor and de-favor certain pathways. This is
reﬂected in the metabolic outcome of the fermentation process. It can be observed in all
processes with a minimum pH, that the adjustment due to acid production performs within
a few hours. Evaluation is based on, whether the change in the initial pH has an eﬀect on
the metabolic performance or not.
A continuous setpoint generally performs better in terms of ethanol production and other
process metrics, speciﬁcally yield, compared to setting a minimum pH. Slightly higher
yields are based on increased ﬁnal ethanol concentrations and decreased by-product con-
centrations.
A stable pH parameter facilitates stability within the growing culture and a clear diﬀerenti-
ation between growth and production phase. Growth of a certain part of the consortium is
Page 55 of 176
==PAGE:57==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
favored and correspondingly its production of a range of fermentation products. Variation
in pH, can alter the microbial composition during the process, as the initial pH favors the
growth of one species and its production. The changing pH leads to a pronounced growth
phase for another organism. This decreases overall ethanol production, as a higher per-
centage of fermentation time and substrate resource is devoted to cell biomass production.
Furthermore, continuous setpoints of pH 5.5, 6.0, 6.5 and 7.0 can be compared in terms
of substrate degradation, productivity, product and by-product concentration and yield
in gram ethanol per gram glycerol consumed. Figure 19 shows comparative plots for the
diﬀerent process metrics over time at diﬀerent pH values.
Page 56 of 176
==PAGE:58==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
(a) Degradation of glycerol in g/L/h over fer-
mentation time in h for pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0,
6.5 and 7.0.
(b) Productivity of ethanol in g/L/h over fer-
mentation time in h for pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0,
6.5 and 7.0.
(c) Ethanol concentration in g/L over fermen-
tation time in h for pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0, 6.5
and 7.0.
(d) Yields in g-ethanol/g-glycerol over fermen-
tation time in h for pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0, 6.5
and 7.0.
(e) 1,3-PDO concentration in g/L over fermen-
tation time in h for pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0, 6.5
and 7.0.
Figure 19: Comparison of glycerol degradation [g/L/h], ethanol productivity [g/L/h], ethanol production
[g/L], yield [g-ethanol/g-glycerol] and concentration of 1,3-PDO [g/L], at pH setpoints of 5.5, 6.0, 6.5 and
7.0, respectively.
Degradation of glycerol (Figure 19a) shows no notable diﬀerences between pH 6.5 and
7.0; for lower pH values of 6.0 and 5.5, the maximum degradation rate is signiﬁcantly
lower and the substrate consumption process is slower. Productivity of ethanol shows an
optimum at pH 6.5, as both lower and higher pH values show a decrease in maximum
productivity. After full glycerol consumption, productivity rates at all pH values drop
Page 57 of 176
==PAGE:59==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
to zero. The resulting ethanol concentration reached over time (Figure 19c) reﬂects the
tendency shown in productivity.
Low and high pH values of 5.5 and 7.0 respectively
show lower maximum ethanol concentration. Fermentation at pH 6.0 and 6.5 both show
similar maximum concentrations of ethanol in the fermentation broth; processing at pH
6.5 shows that it reaches this concentration earlier in the process due to its increased
productivity. Figure 19d shows, that within the ﬁrst 12 hours of fermentation, lower pH
values perform better compared to high pH values. Over 24 hours and until the end of
fermentation, fermentation at pH 6.5 shows improved yields compared to other pH values.
Since the yield of production is only relevant after full substrate conversion, pH 6.5 shows
an advantage compared to lower values. This can be explained by the concentration of
1,3-PDO as the major by-product; monitored results are shown comparatively in Figure
19e.
Lower pH values show a slower production of 1,3-PDO; after 24 to 48 hours of
fermentation; fermentation at pH 6.5 shows signiﬁcantly lower by-product concentration
compared to higher or lower pH values, explaining the higher ethanol-per-glycerol yield.
Performance diﬀerences at diﬀerent pH values can be explained by two diﬀerent factors.
The ﬁrst one is the growth optimum, which was mentioned before. Most species present
survive at a wide range of pH, but still present diﬀerent optima. Besides this, the pH
dependency glycerol fermentation by MM-EF is anticipated, as present species have been
shown to individually ferment substrates in a pH-dependent manner in other studies [37].
Basis of this behaviour is the availability of CO2, which is required for an eﬀective glycerol
fermentation. As visible in the pathway outlined in the literature study, CO2 is produced
from the oxidation of formic acid by a formate hydrogen lyase (FHL) [15]. The enzyme
is only transcribed during fermentative growth and slightly acidic pH. Its activity is also
pH dependent; high pH leads to low FHL activity and corresponding low CO2 generation,
whereas a lower pH facilitates high FHL activity and corresponding high CO2 generation.
Diﬀerences are based on the eﬀect of pH on the equilibrium fractions of CO2 and HCO−
3 .
At high pH, low amounts of CO2 are present in the medium, as HCO−
3 is the main species.
This low CO2 availability is problematic for the cells, as the species transported across the
cell membrane is CO2 and not HCO−
3 . The optimum pH determined in this experiment of
pH 6.5 is thereby backed up, as it is similar to the pKa of the equilibrium reaction between
CO2 and HCO−
3 (pKa = 6.3) [37]. Evaluation of fermentation diﬀerences between pH 6.3
and 6.5 has not been considered in this study.
Fermentation of crude glycerol to ethanol is continued at pH 6.5, because of faster degra-
dation rate, higher productivity, high ﬁnal ethanol concentration reached within shorter
time and high yields due to lower by-product production.
Page 58 of 176
==PAGE:60==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
4.2
Screening for gas ﬂow rate
Figure 20 details the screening for gas ﬂow rates as the second step of the overall ex-
perimental procedure. The improved pH setpoint 6.5 is adopted from the ﬁrst screening
step.
Continuous gas sparging promotes the stripping of fermentation gases from the
process and has experimentally and statistically proven to increase the ethanol yield in
previous research [15]. Considered ﬂow rates include no gas sparging (0.0 L-N2/min), 0.15
L-N2/min, 0.5 L-N2/min, 1.0 L-N2/min and 1.5 L-N2/min.
Figure 20: Status of the experimental Process.
Measured ethanol concentrations are corrected by estimated losses through evaporation,
in accordance with Appendix D; given values account for the total ethanol production.
The processes are evaluated under the premise of complete and successful ethanol captur-
ing. Cell concentration is monitored by measurements of optical density and converted
according to the calibration setup in Appendix F. Detailed raw data on all metabolite
measurements and fermentation conditions can be found in Appendix C.
4.2.1

maximum of 2.76 g-dm/L after 24 hours. pH and cell biomass concentration are shown
together with glycerol, ethanol and 1,3-PDO concentration over time in Figure 21 and
given in detail with other metabolites and calculated process metrics in Table 14.

Figure 26:
Comparison of glycerol degradation [g/L/h], ethanol productivity [g/L/h], total ethanol
production [g/L], yield [g-ethanol/g-glycerol] and concentration of 1,3-PDO [g/L], at nitrogen sparging
rates of 0.0 L/min, 0.15 L/min, 0.5 L/min, 1.0 L/min and 1.5 L/min, respectively.
Glycerol degradation is not signiﬁcantly inﬂuenced by the gas ﬂow rate applied; no gas
sparging signiﬁcantly lowers the rate. Productivity of ethanol increases signiﬁcantly for
Page 67 of 176
==PAGE:69==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
no sparging up to 0.5 L/min; afterwards a less pronounced increase in productivity is vis-
ible with increasing gas ﬂowrate. The total ethanol production achieved increases with
increasing gas ﬂow rate; diﬀerences are more pronounced up to 0.5 L/min, afterwards,
diﬀerences get smaller, suggesting a decreasing eﬀect with a further increase of gas ﬂow
rate. The resulting yields in g/g increase with increasing gas ﬂow rate and are highest for
fermentation at 1.5 L/min nitrogen sparging. By-product production is minimized with
increasing gas sparging ﬂowrate; above 0.5 L/min concentration diﬀerences get less pro-
nounced. As the reductive part of the fermentation pathway is necessary for any oxidative
production of ethanol and other products, there is a limit to which the by-product con-
centration can be reduced. The gas phase exchange and correspondingly its composition
do make an impact on the fermentation output based on observed results. Hydrogen has
shown to be detrimental to the glycerol fermentation process. During fermentation it is
produced in the same pathway as ethanol and proportional to CO2. Suﬃcient agitation
already ensures to avoid hydrogen accumulation within the liquid. At the liquid-gas inter-
face, an accumulation of hydrogen at insuﬃcient gas sparging rates can impair the process
and decrease yields. Fermentation is thereby prevented by fast accumulation of hydrogen,
which is a growth associated product and known to be produced by the consortium of this
study. Details on amounts cannot be made, as it was not quantiﬁed. The inhibitory eﬀect
of hydrogen is based on its negative feedback into the process and is associated with the
reduction of fumarate to succinate. During glycerol fermentation, hydrogen is generated by
the breakdown of formate into CO2 and H2, catalyzed by FHL. Accumulated presence of
H2 allows it to act as an electron donor for the conversion of fumarate to succinate. Since
the conversion of succinate is usually redox-balanced, the presence of an external electron
donor creates an excess of reducing equivalents. this redox imbalance then demonstrates
a negative eﬀect on the ethanol production [30].
Continuous sparging promotes the stripping of fermentation gases, signiﬁcantly improving
the eﬃciency of the glycerol fermentation. Increased sparging however increases utility
consumption; a trade-oﬀcost analysis has thereby to be carried out to determine the up-
scale applicability. High ﬂow-rates also make the trapping of ethanol more diﬃcult, as it
is a very volatile fermentation product [38]. Further experiments are evaluated under the
premise of successful ethanol trapping measures.
Based on experimental results, fermentation is further performed at a pH of 6.5 and a gas
ﬂowrate of 1.5 L-N2/min.

==PAGE:70==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
4.3
Initial Substrate Concentration Screening
Figure 27 shows this part of the project as the third step of lab-scale screening. Optimized
pH setpoint 6.5 and a gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min are adopted from previous steps.
Figure 27: Status of the experimental Process.
Diﬀerent initial glycerol concentrations of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L were tested.
These were estimated based on the composition characterization of crude glycerol, given
the eﬀective percentage of glycerine. Measured ethanol concentrations are corrected by
estimated losses through evaporation, in accordance with loss estimation setups, outlined
in Appendix D; given values account for the total ethanol production. Cell concentration is
monitored by measurements of optical density and converted according to the calibration
setup in Appendix F. Detailed raw data on all metabolite measurements and fermentation
conditions can be found in Appendix C.
