4.3.5
Discussion of screening of initial substrate concentration
The initial substrate concentration aﬀects the process performance. Figure 32 presents an
overview over diﬀerent process parameters over time of fermentation and compares them
with regards to the initial substrate concentration.
(a) Degradation of glycerol in g/L/h over fer-
mentation time in h for initial substrate concen-
trations of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L.
(b) Productivity of ethanol in g/L/h over fer-
mentation time in h for initial substrate concen-
trations of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L.
(c) Ethanol production in g/L over fermentation
time in h for initial substrate concentrations of
10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L.
(d) Yields in g-ethanol/g-glycerol over fermen-
tation time in h for initial substrate concentra-
tions of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L.
(e) 1,3-PDO concentration in g/L over fermen-
tation time in h for initial substrate concentra-
tions of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L.
Figure 32:
Comparison of glycerol degradation [g/L/h], ethanol productivity [g/L/h], total ethanol
production [g/L], yield [g-ethanol/g-glycerol] and concentration of 1,3-PDO [g/L], at initial substrate
concentrations of 10 g/L, 15 g/L, 20 g/L and 35 g/L, respectively.
Page 75 of 176
==PAGE:77==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
For each parameter, diﬀerences are notable, depending on the initial glycerol concentration.
Degradation of glycerol (Figure 32a) increases with increasing glycerol concentration; at
the same time the maximum degradation rate is reached later in the process, when all
glycerol is consumed. Full glycerol consumption takes an increasing amount of time with
increasing initial concentration. Productivity of ethanol (Figure 32b) shows a diﬀerent
behaviour. From 10 g/L to 15 g/L, the maximum productivity increases; 20 g/L initial
concentration shows no notable diﬀerence in the productivity. A further increase to 35 g/L
initial concentration shows a decrease in maximum ethanol productivity and a delayed
peak production. Figure 32c shows that with an increasing initial glycerol concentration,
a higher total ethanol production is reached. An increase from 10 g/L up to 20 g/L shows
high diﬀerences within the ethanol concentration reached; a further increase of glycerol
concentration to 35 g/L shows a less pronounced increase in ethanol production, since
the maximum concentration stays approximately the same compared to 20 g/L initial
glycerol concentration. The yield in gram ethanol produced per gram glycerol consumed
(Figure 32d shows decreasing yields with increasing initial glycerol concentration. This is
correlated with the by-product production, namely 1,3-PDO, as shown in Figure 32e. As
visible, at initial glycerol concentrations above 15 g/L, the production of 1,3-PDO increases
signiﬁcantly, lowering the yield of ethanol per glycerol consumed.
The observed behavior can be explained based on the reductive part of the fermentation
pathway. Glycerol enters the microbial cell by diﬀusion and is then in the reductive part of
the pathway dehydrated to 3-HP, which is then further reduced to 1,3-PDO (or 1,2-PDO)
[39]. An intolerance due to high levels of diol concentrations is possible, but in this case not
probably, as the inhibitory limit for such is reported as 65 g/L [40], which is not reached in
this case. An excess of glycerol availability, as it is promoted with an increasing initial sub-
strate concentration, increases the activity of glycerol dehydrogenase, which is responsible
for the conversion of glycerol to 3-HP [39]. The further conversion of 3-HP to 1,3-PDO
(or 1,2-PDO) is catalyzed by the coenzyme B12-dependent glycerol dehydratase, which is
the rate-limiting step, especially in processes without excessive nutrient supplementation.
At 3-HP levels above 30 mM, is inhibited, undergoing an irreversible suicide inactivation
[40]. This type of inhibition associated with an accumulation of 3-HP during glycerol fer-
mentation has also been previously observed with K. pneumoniae and C. freundii [41].
An increased yield and process performance at lower initial glycerol concentrations is also
observed in other monoculture fermentation of glycerol [30].
The initial substrate concentration cannot be inﬁnitely increased, as the production of
by-products increases, and intermediates accumulate in the fermentation broth, impairing
ethanol production and resulting in signiﬁcantly lower yields. 15 g/L is chosen as the initial
substrate concentration to proceed with, based on high yields and productivity.
Page 76 of 176
==PAGE:78==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
4.4
Screening of Feeding Strategy
After the ﬁrst three steps of screening, improved process conditions are adapted, as shown
in Figure 33. The process is now evaluated under fed-batch operation, to compare with
previous batch processes under the same conditions (pH setpoint, gas sparging rate and
initial substrate concentration).
Figure 33: Status of the experimental Process.
Due to a lack of data points in this stage of the experimental process, it was not possible to
evaluate each of the fed-batch setups individually. Any conclusions drawn would be based
on too many assumptions, in order for them to be accurate.
A comparative approach was used, to evaluate whether a fed-batch process can live up to
and increase process metrics, compared to the respective batch process.
The ﬁrst fed-batch setup was carried out at pH setpoint 6.5 and no gas sparging. The
process is started at an initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L; every 12 hours the process
is fed to a glycerol concentration of 15 g/L. This setup is compared to the batch process
run at pH setpoint 6.5, no gas sparging and an initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L.
Purpose of this comparison was to see how far the measured ethanol concentration in the
fermentation broth could be increased, before inhibitory patterns would be visible. At no
gas sparging, estimated losses through evaporation showed to be insigniﬁcant. It comes at
the trade-oﬀof a lower production yield.
The second fed-batch setup was conducted at pH setpoint 6.5 and a nitrogen sparging rate
of 1.5 L-N2/min. The process is started at an initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L;
every 9 hours the process is fed to a glycerol concentration of 15 g/L. This is compared to
the batch fermentation run at pH setpoint 6.5, a gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min and
an initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L. Purpose of this comparison is the potential
increase of the process yield and ethanol productivity.
This process has only limited
potential to increase the measured ethanol concentration in the fermentation broth, as
continuous gas sparging and an increasing ethanol concentration, proportionally increase
evaporative losses of ethanol.
Page 77 of 176
==PAGE:79==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
The evaluation of the process is focused on glycerol and ethanol concentrations. A con-
stant glycerol degradation rate is assumed, which was calculated from the batch process.
Based on this degradation rate and the known amount of glycerol added in each feeding
cycle, a theoretical "Calculated Glycerol Concentration" can be estimated for each time
point during the fermentation. Plotting this curve together with data points of measured
glycerol concentrations during fed-batch operation shows, when the process starts divert-
ing negatively or positively. From that time point on, the processes cannot be compared
anymore, without making too many assumptions, leading to insuﬃcient conclusions.
For the time where the measured glycerol concentration aligns with the predicted "Calcu-
lated Glycerol Concentration", a constant degradation rate is assumed for the fed-batch
process. Multiplying the glycerol degradation rate with the yield of ethanol produced per
glycerol consumed Yg/g during batch operation, results in a predicted ethanol production
rate. From this, a "Calculated Total Ethanol Production" can be calculated. Since ethanol
losses follow ﬁrst order kinetics and are dependent on the measured ethanol concentration
in the liquid, a "Calculated Ethanol Concentration" can be predicted (Calculations can be
found in Appendix D and G). By comparing this calculated ethanol concentration to ac-
tual measured data of ethanol concentration in the liquid, it can be seen when the process
starts to divert negatively or positively. This leads to conclusions on higher or lower yields
of ethanol production of the fed-batch process, compared to the batch process.
Detailed raw data on all metabolite measurements and fermentation conditions can be
found in Appendix C.
4.4.1
Fed-Batch with 0.0 L-N2/min
Figure 34 shows batch-process-based predictions for glycerol concentrations, total ethanol
production and calculated ethanol concentration in the liquid, compared to measured dat-
apoints of glycerol and ethanol concentration during fed-batch operation.

Figure 34: Comparative evaluation of fed-batch to batch operation with regards to glycerol and ethanol
concentration, evaluating glycerol degradation rate and yield of ethanol produced per glycerol consumed.
No gas sparging, feeding every 12 hours to a glycerol concentration of 15 g/L and pH setpoint 6.5.
Figure 34a shows the calculated glycerol concentration, based on a constant degradation
rate of 1.5408 g/L/h, obtained during the compared batch operation and a known amount
added per feeding cycle. This is compared to measured data points of glycerol concentration
during fed-batch fermentation. It can be seen that during the ﬁrst 48 hours of fermentation,
corresponding to four completed feeding cycles, measurements align with the predicted
graph. Afterwards, glycerol starts accumulating and diverts from the estimates. During
the ﬁrst 48 hours, a constant glycerol degradation rate can be assumed for the basis of
further evaluation.
Figure 34b shows the calculated total ethanol production, which is based on a constant
glycerol degradation rate of 1.5408 g/L/h and a process yield of Yg/g=0.14 (obtained
during the compared batch process). Considering evaporative losses, a calculated ethanol
concentration is predicted. Since no gas sparging is performed, losses are insigniﬁcant.
Comparing to the data points of ethanol concentration in the liquid during fed-batch
fermentation shows that measurements ﬁt to the calculated value for the ﬁrst 12 hours of
fermentation time. During this time, the process thereby produces ethanol at the same
yield as in batch operation.
After 12 hours, the measured concentration of ethanol is
signiﬁcantly lower than the calculated estimate, suggesting lower yields of production.
Monitoring of other metabolites shows that those production rates and yields do not change
signiﬁcantly compared to batch operation. Detailed data on other metabolite concentra-
tions over time can be found in Appendix C.

Figure 35: Comparative evaluation of fed-batch to batch operation with regards to glycerol and ethanol
concentration, evaluating glycerol degradation rate and yield of ethanol produced per glycerol consumed.
1.5 L/min gas sparging, feeding every 9 hours to a glycerol concentration of 15 g/L and pH setpoint 6.5.
Figure 35a shows the calculated glycerol concentration, which is based on a constant degra-
dation rate of 2.1425 g/L/h, which was obtained from operation under batch conditions.
Comparing this curve to measured data points of glycerol concentration in the liquid during
fed-batch operation shows that the measured concentrations ﬁrst diverge from predictions
after 36 hours of fermentation. Afterwards, glycerol accumulation due to a changed degra-
dation rate is visible. During the ﬁrst 36 hours, a constant degradation rate can be assumed
for the fed-batch process. For the ﬁrst 36 hours of fermentation, an estimated total ethanol
production is calculated, as shown in Figure 35b. Based on a constant glycerol degradation
rate of 2.1425 g/L/h and an ethanol production yield of Yg/g=0.37 (obtained from batch
process), an ethanol production rate and a corresponding total ethanol production are cal-
culated. Considering evaporative losses, a calculated ethanol concentration is predicted.
Since a high rate of gas sparging is applied, losses are signiﬁcant and get more pronounced
with an increasing ethanol concentration in the liquid. This "Calculated Ethanol Concen-
tration" is compared to measured concentrations of ethanol during fed-batch operation.
After 27 hours, equal to three ﬁnished feeding cycles, measured ethanol concentrations start
negatively diverting from calculated values, suggesting a decreased yield. Other measured
metabolites do not change in their production rate or yield (Appendix C).

==PAGE:82==

Christina Etler
4.4.3
Discussion of Fed-Batch Results
Fed-batch operation without application of gas sparging was performed for the purpose
of increasing the measured ethanol concentration in the liquid. At no gas sparging, esti-
mated losses through evaporation showed to be insigniﬁcant. A trade-oﬀof a lower ethanol
production yield was accepted for this purpose. Data showed that glycerol degradation
can be assumed constant for 48 hours of fermentation, at the same value as observed
in batch fermentation. Comparing estimated and measured ethanol concentration within
this time shows a negative divergence of measured values from estimates after 12 hours of
fermentation time, suggesting lower production yields. Potential cause of this inhibition
pattern could be the accumulation of hydrogen at the liquid-gas interface. This was also
observed during batch operation; with repeated feeding after 12 hours, hydrogen accumu-
lation reaches even higher concentrations, impairing the fermentation performance.
Fed-batch operation with a gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min had the purpose of poten-
tially increasing the process yield of ethanol production. It had only a limited potential
to increase the measured ethanol concentration in the fermentation broth, as continuous
gas sparging and an increasing ethanol concentration, proportionally increase evaporative
losses of ethanol. Data showed that the glycerol degradation rate can be assumed constant
for the ﬁrst 36 hours of fermentation, at the same value as obtained in respective batch
fermentation. Measured ethanol concentrations within this time align with calculated esti-
mates within the ﬁrst 27 hours of fermentation; afterwards they start negatively diverting,
anticipating an inhibition problem.
Since crude glycerol is fed, besides glycerol, other
compounds are added, which are not consumed in the process and start accumulating.
These include among others, especially methanol and free fatty acids. Since free fatty acid
content of this speciﬁc substrate is not analyzed, any evaluation would be speculative; it is
though a potential inhibition source. Based on the known content of <1% methanol, it has
accumulated to a concentration of approximately 0.5-0.6 g/L in the broth after 27 hours.
Since inhibitory limits are reported from 0.4 g/L, this could be inhibiting the process [42].
To make a more detailed evaluation, the substrate has to be analyzed with regards to free
fatty acid content.
In both cases, the fed-batch fermentation can only live up to batch operation within a
time of 12 and 27 hours, respectively. Under the premise of increasing yield and product
concentration, this deﬂects the purpose and has to be considered not successful. Especially
for high ﬂow rates, calculations predict, that only a limited concentration of ethanol can be
reached in the broth, far below inhibitory concentrations. Limitations of this setup include
the insuﬃcient amount of data points, which limits evaluation. The concept of fed-batch
operation has to be approached diﬀerently for this process to avoid observed problems.
Page 81 of 176
==PAGE:83==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
4.5
Pilot-scale Validation
Final step of the experiments of this project was the validation and up-scaling of improved
parameters into pilot-scale (Figure 36). Since fed-batch fermentation did not yield any
improvements in its current setup, only the batch process under improved conditions was
scaled up.
Figure 36: Status of the experimental Process.
A pilot-scale batch setup is performed to validate the results obtained in lab-scale. Process
conditions include a temperature of 37 °C, pH setpoint 6.5, controlled by 2M KOH and
2M H2SO4, an initial substrate concentration of 15 g/L (aimed for) and a ﬂowrate of 22 L-
N2/min. The gas ﬂow rate of 1.5 L/min in lab-scale is scaled up based on the active liquid
fermentation volume. Measured ethanol concentrations are corrected by estimated losses
through evaporation, in accordance with Appendix D; given values account for the total
ethanol production. Cell concentration is monitored by measurements of optical density
and converted according to the calibration setup in Appendix F. Detailed raw data on all
metabolite measurements and fermentation conditions can be found in Appendix C.
Figure 37 shows stable fermentation conditions with regards to pH, details can be found
in Appendix C. Cell concentration reaches a maximum of 2.86 g-dm/L after 8 hours. It
gives additionally an overview on substrate concentration and main metabolite production
over time; Table 23 details metabolite production and calculated process metrics.

As visible from Table 23, the targeted initial glycerol concentration of 15 g/L was not
achieved, but only 10 g/L were added, probably due to in-homogeneous substrate and
insuﬃcient mixing before addition. The present glycerol is fully degraded within 6-8 hours
at a maximum degradation rate of 2.35 g/L/h. Ethanol is produced at a maximum pro-
ductivity of 0.69 g/L/h, resulting in a maximum total ethanol production of 3.33 g/L after
48 hours. This includes both the measured concentration in the fermentation broth and
estimated evaporative losses. Ethanol production per glycerol consumed corresponds to a
maximum yield of 0.34 g/g after 48 hours, 0.68 mole/mole, respectively. Compared to this,
the lab-scale batch-process showed a maximum ethanol production of 2.51 g/L (after 12h)
and 2.25 g/L (after 48h), corresponding to a yield of maximum 0.27 g/g after 12 hours.
The up-scaled process shows an improved performance. Reasons for the positive change
Page 83 of 176
==PAGE:85==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
could be the mode of up-scaling the gas ﬂow rate. This only considered the active liquid
fermentation volume. The diﬀerence in the ratio of liquid volume to surface area, as well
as the head space volume have not been considered. Considering that the up-scaled gas
sparging rate was over-estimated suggests that the inﬂuence of this parameter needs to be
subject to further research, both in lab- and pilot-scale.

5
Conclusion
This section addresses the outcome of this project and highlights limitations and future
research.
Utilization of crude glycerol waste-streams as a by-product of BDF production remains
a challenge to overcome to ensure future economic viability of biodiesel production pro-
cesses. Due to cost-intensive puriﬁcation of glycerol-containing waste streams, options for
valorizing the crude substrate are explored. Mixed microbial cultures show to be a promis-
ing alternative, as they eliminate many of the industrial microbial challenges, including
pathogenicity, contamination, temperature and pH conditions. They are able to work un-
der non-sterile conditions and due to their complex metabolism they can utilize complex
carbon substrates such as glycerol. Optimizing process conditions is especially important,
when working with MMCs, as those are directly reﬂected in the culture composition and
corresponding metabolic output.
Main target of this project was the improvement of pH, gas sparging rate and initial sub-
strate concentration. Results were evaluated under batch and fed-batch feeding strategies
and validated in pilot-scale.
Figure 38: Overview on the experimental process, including the main screening results.
Figure 38 shows an overview on the experimental procedure in this study and highlights
the main results from the ﬁrst three steps of screening. pH screening showed improved
performance at a continuous setpoint at pH 6.5, controlled by 2M KOH and 2M H2SO4.
Gas sparging rates of nitrogen of 1.5 L-N2/min reduced by-product production and in-
creased ﬁnal ethanol concentrations and yields.
Economic analysis has to be done for
up-scale applicability, as this increased production comes at the trade-oﬀof higher utility
consumption. 15 g/L initial glycerol concentration showed to be most economically fea-
sible, as yields of ethanol produced per glycerol consumed were highest. Further increase
of substrate concentration does increase the ﬁnal ethanol concentration, but decreases the
process yield.
Total improved process conditions resulted in a maximum yield of Yg/g = 0.37 (gram
Page 85 of 176
==PAGE:87==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
ethanol produced per gram glycerol consumed with a total ethanol production of 4.93 g/L
after 48 hours.
Noticeable is that under certain conditions, the maximum ethanol concentration is already
reached after 12-24 hours; further operation shows a production decrease. Potential cause
is the total glycerol absence at this point in the process, leading to ethanol being consumed
as a substrate by some microorganisms. Future research can consider shortening the total
fermentation time.
Fed-batch operation without application of gas sparging had the purpose of increasing the
measured ethanol concentration in the liquid, since estimated losses through evaporation
would be insigniﬁcant. A trade-oﬀof a lower ethanol production yield was accepted for
this purpose.
Based on glycerol degradation and ethanol yields, a negative divergence
of measured values from calculated estimates is observed after 12 hours of fermentation,
suggesting lower production yields. Accumulation of hydrogen at the liquid-gas interface
is presumably impairing the process. An increase of ethanol concentration in the liquid
compared to the batch process cannot be reached.
Fed-batch operation with a gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min was performed for the pur-
pose of increasing the process yield of ethanol production. Based on glycerol degradation
and comparing measured ethanol concentrations to calculated estimates, the process nega-
tively diverts from batch process performance after 27 hours of fermentation, anticipating
and inhibition problem. Accumulating methanol and free fatty acids from the crude glyc-
erol are estimated to reach inhibitory limits, inhibiting the process.
In both cases, the fed-batch fermentation can only live up to batch operation within a
limited time frame of 12 and 27 hours, respectively.
Under the premise of increasing
yield and product concentration, this deﬂects the purpose and has to be considered not
successful. Limitation of this evaluation is the lack of data points throughout the fed-batch
fermentation, since it only allows comparison and no detailed individual evaluation. To
back up inhibition estimated, a more detailed characterization of the substrate is necessary.
Improved process conditions in batch fermentation are validated in pilot-scale and show
even higher potential as estimated based on lab-scale results. Reasons for the positive
divergence can be found in the method of up-scaling the gas sparging ﬂowrate. This did
not consider the head space volume and the surface-to-volume ratio of the liquid. Positive
divergence of results from estimates emphasizes, that this parameter has the potential for
further improvement.
This project has improved the crude glycerol fermentation process by mixed microbial
consortia to a pH setpoint of 6.5, an initial glycerol concentration of 15 g/L and a continuous
gas sparging rate of 1.5 L-N2/min. Maximum yields of Yg/g = 0.37 are achieved with a
Page 86 of 176
==PAGE:88==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
total ethanol production of 4.93 g/L after 48 hours. These results emphasize the potential
of applying mixed microbial consortia in crude glycerol conversion technologies.
Besides the time limitation, insuﬃcient substrate characterization proved to be a limita-
tion in this process; it is to be evaluated in future research. Fed-batch operation showed
inhibitory patterns, which could have potentially been predicted before. The concept has
to be improved for a potentially increased process performance.
Next steps of future research include the optimization of gas sparging rates both in lab- and
pilot-scale, as it showed potential for further improvement. The transfer from anaerobic
conditions to a microaerophilic environment can additionally be considered, as this has
previously shown slightly increased process yields [36]. As all processes in this project are
evaluated under the premise of successful capturing of all ethanol losses, this also has to
be addressed further in a series of experimental work. Increased productivity and yields
need to be combined with a high concentration of ethanol in liquid form to be able to
industrially utilize the process.

Appendix A - Thesis Statement
Liquid biofuels have had a breakthrough over the past years; an increasing industrial pro-
duction, which is expected to increase further in the coming years, leads to an extensive
amount of unused by-products. Among such, crude glycerol represents the majority. It is
less investigated compared to reﬁned glycerol, as it is limited by the presence of diﬀerent
other components, diﬀering on the substrate source. These lead to decreased substrate
homogeneity and the formation of potentially inhibitory compounds, which makes feasible
large scale utilization diﬃcult. A solution to the problem could be mixed microbial cul-
tures, as they show a complementary metabolism, utilizing multiple carbon sources and
the ability to degrade complex substrates, such as glycerol. Depending on the overall mi-
crobial composition and fermentation conditions, diﬀerent microbial strains dominate the
fermentation, resulting in diﬀerent dominant products to be obtained.
This Master Project is carried out in continuation of the GRAIL EU project, aiming to
contribute to the development of an optimized bioreﬁnery concept for the fermentation of
ethanol from crude glycerol.
The work consists of literature- and an experimental-based parts. The literature study
focuses on recent advances in bio-diesel production and their main by-products, which
include crude glycerol. It elaborates on the potential bioreﬁnery applications of such as
substrate and the potential of mixed microbial cultures as processing microorganisms.
Screening in a previous project for diﬀerent mixed microbial cultures and media under
anaerobic and microaerophilic conditions is included, which is backed up by the results of
the special course in Fall 2017 Mixed microbial cultures under anaerobic and microaerophilic
conditions for pilot-scale fermentation of crude glycerol to ethanol.
The experimental part of the study consists essentially of three diﬀerent parts: (1) Lab-scale
screening of pH, (2) Lab-scale screening of initial substrate concentration and (3) valida-
tion under diﬀerent feeding strategies in pilot-scale. Its purpose is the detailed screening
and optimization of pH setpoints and initial substrate concentration. Screenings are car-
ried out in laboratory scale and consequently veriﬁed in pilot scale and evaluated under
diﬀerent feeding strategies. Optimized fermentation conditions with regards to pH, aera-
tion, inoculum, medium and initial substrate concentration are pursuing to maximize the
overall ethanol yield per glycerol, while ensuring full substrate consumption. This paves
the path to an economically feasible technology, suitable for industrial application. Good
Laboratory Practice (GLP) is trained and respected throughout the experimental work.
All experimental work is carried out at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in the
Chemical Engineering Institute. The project is supervised by associate professors Hariklia
N. Gavala and Ioannis V. Skiadas.
Page 94 of 176
==PAGE:96==
Crude glycerol to ethanol fermentation
Christina Etler
The eﬀect of ethanol discharge through the oﬀ-gas by evaporation has to be accounted
for upon evaluation of results. Ethanol stripping can also be modelled as a form of prod-
uct separation, but in this case is not optimized but considered at speciﬁed operating
conditions. Obtained results are corrected to estimate the total ethanol production and
corresponding yields.
Besides the scientiﬁc outcome of this project, diﬀerent types of analysis equipment and
reactor systems both in laboratory and pilot scale are familiarized with. Organizational
and decision-making skills are developed; report writing and appropriate presentation of
scientiﬁc results are included. Furthermore, the project will result in an updated fermenter
manual for fermenter 14 A/B (pilot plant, building 228, DTU).
Milestones are deﬁned as reasonable by the content of the thesis and the division of exper-
imental parts. They are deﬁned as (I) Literature Study, (II) Screening and (III) validation
of lab-scale results in pilot-scale under diﬀerent feeding strategies.
Deadlines for the corresponding status reports are set to be (I) 30.02.2018, (II) 31.03.2018
and (III) 15.06.2018 (ﬁnal deadline) . This also outlines a time line for the experimental
work. The project is to be ﬁnalized no later than the 15.06.18.

Description of chemicals/products (Relevant issues from SDS, section D) 
E.g. physical state, vapor pressure and compatibility 
Colourless hygroscopic liquid, molecular weight 60.05 g/mol, density 1.05 g/cm3, miscible with water under any 
conditions, miscible with ethanol, glycerol, diethyl ether and carbon tetrachloride, practically insoluble in carbon 
disulfide, pH 2.4 (6%), melting point 16-16.5C, boiling point 117-118C, flash point 40C, vapour pressure 11.4 
mmhg20c, autoignition temperature 485C, explosive limit 4-19.9 % v/v, threshold limit value TLV=25 mg/m3, vapour 
hazard index 2070. 
Materials to avoid: Oxidizing agents, acids, bases, carbonates and phosphates, hydroxides, alcohols, metals, oxides, 
peroxides, permanganates, amines. Moisture. In contact with strong oxidizing agent fire and explosions may occur. 
When diluting concentrated acid it must always be poured into water under refrigeration. Pouring water into acid may 
cause an explosive reaction. 
Acids react with a number of metals to produce hydrogen which, in contact with the air, may cause explosions. 
Acidification of the external environment. 
Harmful to aquatic organisms. 
Is potentially biodegradable. 
Not considered to be bio-accumulating. 
Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.  
State approx. concentrations and amounts, etc. Attach the complete protocol if necessary.  
State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if not possible, use a respiratory protective device with 
combination filter ABEK and a particle filter. The choice of filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4.). 
Filtering respiratory protective device must only be used 3 hours a day. 
Use safety goggles. 
Use protective face shield and e.g. apron. 
By direct skin contact use: Silver Shield/4H® (PE/EVAL/PE). 
Penetration time: >3 hours. 
 
==PAGE:103==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
It is recommended to use disposable nitrile gloves in case of spillage. 
Disposable nitrile gloves have a short penetration time for most substances. In case of spillage on the glove, it is 
immediately renewed. Wash your hands thoroughly with soap and water. 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible. 
Flammable substance: never expose or store the substance near open flame. Be aware of the risk of sparking from static 
electricity. Ensure all equipment is earthed. 
Arrangements must be made to avoid contact with the substance. 
Frequency of use 
weekly 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
Evacuate the area as necessary. Switch off all apparatus. Prevent further spreading and ventilate the area.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Soak up any spillages with vermiculite or sand and discard as chemical waste.  
Clean the area after clearing up the spillage.  
Inform the municipal authorities and those responsible for the environment in the workplace of any major discharge into 
the surroundings.  
In case of fire, hazardous decomposition products are formed, e.g. carbon monoxide. Extinguishing via  carbon dioxide 
or dry chemical powder. Be aware that dry chemical powder may damage your apparatus. 
In case of any accident, take the patient to the exergency department at the nearest hospital, as delayed reaction may 
occur. 
In case of inhalation: the person should be taken into the fresh air, kept quiet and under supervision, if there is a risk of 
loss of consciousness the person should be placed in recovery position and kept warm, if the person stops breathing, 
artificial respiration should be given. 
In case of skin contact: rinse thoroughly with water, remove contaminated clothing and any jewellery. 
In case of eye contact: Rinse with water immediately, hold eye wide open, remove contact lenses, continue to rinse with 
water until treatment is taken over by doctors. 
If swallowed: remove any visible particles from in and around the mouth, immediately rinse the mouth out and drink 
water or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, 
taking along these instructions. 
In case of burns: rinse immediately with water and continue until pain disappears, while rinsing with water, remove 
clothing which is not burnt on the skin, go to emergency department at nearest hospital and continue to rinse with water 
en route. 
Collection of waste  (SDS, section F. Evt attach yellow cancer  warning) 
Waste group 
UN no. 
Chemical Waste, Class 8, CF1, PG II 
C 
2789 
Safety instructions for storage  (SDS, section H) 
Original chemicals, stock solutions and products: E.g. sign posting, ventilation, cooling 
Must be stored tightly sealed in a well-ventilated chemical cabinet. 
Protected against moisture. 
Avoid sources of ignition (heat, electrical installations). Special requirements for safety notices apply to the storage of 
large quantities, see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 
518, 1994 and Danish Emergency Management Agency Authority Statutory order on flammable and combustible liquids 
No. 1639 of 06/12/2016 and associated guidance from 19 January 2017.  
 
Flammable liquid, class 
II-2 
There must be stored up to 50 storage units per laboratory or chemical storage, or up to 800 units per fire section. (1 
storage unit corresponds to 5 liter). See Danish Emergency Management Agency Authority Statutory order on 
flammable and combustible liquids No. 1639 of 06/12/2016 and associated guidance from 19 January 2017. 
Must be kept up to a height of 160 cm. Special requirements as to safety notices apply to the storage of large quantities, 
see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 518, 1994. 
Substitution analysis  (SDS, section I)

Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.  
State approx. concentrations and amounts, etc. Attach the complete protocol if necessary.  
State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if not possible, use a respiratory protective device with 
gas filter A and a particle filter. The choice of filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4). Filtering respiratory 
protective must only be used 3 hours a day. Use safety goggles. 
Use protective face shield and e.g. apron. 
It is not possible to find a suitable disposable glove according to the Quick Selection Guide to Chemical Protective 
Clothing (Fifth Edition). It is recommended to use disposal nitrile rubber gloves. The penetration time is not known, but 
is expected to be short. It is recommended that the gloves are to be renewed immediately in case of any spillage. Wash 
your hands thoroughly with soap and water before using new gloves. 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible. 
Arrangements must be made to avoid contact with the substance. 
The substance has a low vapour pressure and will only emit limited vapour. NB: Heating, spraying or other aerosol-
generating work processes considerably increase the risk of inhalation. 
Frequency of use 
weekly 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
==PAGE:114==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
Evacuate the area as necessary. Switch off all apparatus. Prevent further spreading and ventilate the area.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Soak up any spillages with vermiculite or sand and discard as chemical waste.  
Clean the area after clearing up the spillage.  
Inform the municipal authorities and those responsible for the environment in the workplace of any major discharge into 
the surroundings. 
In case of fire, hazardous decomposition products a.o. carbon monoxide are formed. Extinguishing with water spray, 
carbon dioxide or dry chemical powder; be awae that dry chemical powder may damage your apparatus. 
In case of inhalation: the person should be taken into the fresh air, kept quiet and under supervision, if there is a risk of 
loss of consciousness the person should be places in recovery position and kept warm, if the person stops breathing, 
artificial respiration should be given. 
In case of skin contact: rinse thoroughly with water, remove contaminated clothing and any jewellery 
In case of eye contact: immediately rinse with water, hold the eye wide open, remove contact lenses, continue to rinse 
with water until treatment is taken over by doctors. 
If swallowed: remove any visible particles from in and around the mouth, immediately rinse mouth out and drink water 
or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, taking 
along these instructions. 
Collection of waste  (SDS, section F. Evt attach yellow cancer  warning) 
Waste group 
UN no. 
Class 8, C3, PG III 
C 
2829 
Safety instructions for storage  (SDS, section H) 
Original chemicals, stock solutions and products: E.g. sign posting, ventilation, cooling 
Must be stored tightly sealed in a well-ventilated chemical cabinet. 
Special requirements apply to the storage under lock and key in cupboards or rooms which are fitted with warning 
boards (toxic substances). A chemical responsible person must be appointed, Classification Declaration no. 1075 of 
24.11.2011 (§36 and §40). 
 
Must be kept up to a height of 160 cm. Special requirements as to safety notices apply to the storage of large quantities, 
see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 518, 1994.

Description of chemicals/products (Relevant issues from SDS, section D) 
E.g. physical state, vapor pressure and compatibility 
This compound is published at the list of harmonized classification. 
Colourless hygroscopic liquid, molecular weight 46.07 g/mol, density 0.78 g/cm3, water solubility 1000 g/L, miscible 
with diethyl ether, acetone, chloroform, soluble in benzene, pH 7 (1% aq. Soln.), melting point -114C, boiling point 
78.0C, flash point 13C, vapour pressure 59.3 mmHg, autoignition temperature 363C, explosive limit 3.3-19 % v/v, 
threshold limit value TLV=1900 mg/m3, vapour hazard index 71.4. 
Incompatible with a.o. alkali metals, ammonia, oxidizing agents, peroxides, strong acids, acid anhydrides and acid 
chlorides. 
Considered to be readily biodegradable. Not considered bio-accumulating. Is only mildly toxic to aquatic organisms. 
Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.  
State approx. concentrations and amounts, etc. Attach the complete protocol if necessary.  
State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if not possible, use a respiratory protective device with 
gas filter A and a particle filter. The choice of filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4.). 
Filtering respiratory protective device must only be used 3 hours a day. 
Use safety goggles. 
By direct skin contact use recommended disposable gloves from silvershield/4H (PE/PA/PE rubber): Pentration time >3 
hours 
It is recommended to use disposable nitrile gloves in case of spillage. 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible. 
Flammable substance: never expose or store the substance near open flame. Be aware of the risk of sparking from static 
electricity. Ensure all equipment is earthed. 
Be aware that working with the substance may generate an explosive atmosphere. 
At assessing the workplace, special attention must be paid as the substance is harmful or suspedted to be harmful to 
embryo, egg and sperm cells. Moving to another job may be necessary. 
Arrangements must be made to avoid contact with the substance. 
Frequency of use 
daily 
==PAGE:116==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
Evacuate the area as necessary. Switch off all apparatus. Prevent further spreading and ventilate the area.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Soak up any spillages with vermiculite or sand and discard as chemical waste.  
Clean the area after clearing up the spillage.  
Inform the municipal authorities and those responsible for the environment in the workplace of any major discharge into 
the surroundings. 
In case of fire, hazardous decomposition products are formed, e.g. carbon monoxide. Extinguishing by water spray, 
carbon dioxide or dry chemical powder. Be aware that dry chemical powder may damage your apparatus. 
In case of inhalation: The person should be taken into the fresh air, kept quiet and under supervision, if there is a risk of 
loss of consciousness the person should be placed in recovery position and kept warm, if person stops breathing, 
artificial respiration should be given. 
In case of skin contact: rinse thoroughly with water, remove contaminated clothing and any jewellery. 
In case of eye contact: rinse immediately with water, hold the eye wide open, remove contact lenses, continue to rinse 
with water until treatment is taken over by doctors. 
If swallowed: immediately rinse the mouth out and drink water or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not 
cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, taking along these instructions. 
In case of burns: rinse immediately with water and continue until pain disappears, while rinsing with water remove 
clothing which is not burnt on the skin, go to emergency department at nearest hospital and continue to rinse with water 
en route.

Description of chemicals/products (Relevant issues from SDS, section D) 
E.g. physical state, vapor pressure and compatibility 
This compound is published at the list of harmonized classification. White or yellowish crystals (pellets), molecular 
weight 56.11 g/mol, density 2.044 g/cm3, soluble in water, ethanol and glycerol, melting point 406C, boiling point 
1327C, threshold limit value TLV=2 mg/m3. 
Materials to avoid a.o.: aluminium, organic materials, acid chlorides, acid anhydrides, magnesium, copper. Contact 
with aluminium, tin and zinc and their alloys liberates hydrogen. When dissolved in water, strong heat is developed. 
Potassium hydroxide reacts violently with acids developing heat.  
Harmful to flora and fauna. 
Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.  
State approx. concentrations and amounts, etc. Attach the complete protocol if necessary.  
State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Concentration used: 4M 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if this is not possible use a particle filter. The choice of 
filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4.). 
Filtering respiratory protective device must only be used 3 hours a day. 
Use safety goggles. 
Use protective face shield and e.g. apron. 
It is recommended to use disposable nitrile gloves in case of spillage. 
Disposable nitrile gloves have a short penetration time for most substances. In case of spillage on the glove, it is 
immediately renewed. Wash your hands thoroughly with soap and water. 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible. 
Arrangements must be made to avoid contact with the substance. 
Frequency of use 
weekly 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
==PAGE:126==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
Limit the generation of dust particles. Keep unauthorised persons away and announce any increased risk of adverse 
effects.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Clear up the spillage, with damp cloth if appropriate. Discard the cloth and spillage as chemical waste in tightly sealed 
container. Clean the area after clearing up the spillage.  
Inform the municipal authorities and those responsible for the environment in the workplace of any major discharge into 
the surroundings. 
In case of fire, the substance is noncombustible and decomposes on heating to hazardous products, e.g. potassium 
oxides and hydrogen. 
In case of any accident, take the patient to emergency department at the nearest hospital, as delayed reaction may 
occur. 
In case of inhalation: the person should be taken into the fresh air, kept quiet and under supervision, if there is a risk of 
loss of consciousness the person should be placed in recovery position and kept warm, if the person stops breathing, 
artificial respiration should be given. 
In case of skin contact: rinse thoroughly with water, remove contaminated clothing and any jewellery; caustic burns 
may need to be rinsed for up to 24 hours. 
In case of eye contact: Rinse with water immediately, hold eye wide open, remove contact lenses, continue to rinse with 
water until treatment is taken over by doctors. 
If swallowed: remove any visible particles from in and around the mouth, immediately rinse the mouth out and drink 
water or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, 
taking along these instructions. 
Collection of waste  (SDS, section F. Evt attach yellow cancer  warning) 
Waste group 
UN no. 
Chemical Waste, Class 8, C6, PG II 
X 
1813 
Safety instructions for storage  (SDS, section H) 
Original chemicals, stock solutions and products: E.g. sign posting, ventilation, cooling 
Must be stored tightly sealed in a well-ventilated chemical cabinet. 
Protectet against moist. Not together with acids. Absorbs carbon dioxide from air. 
Do never use galvanic packaging (forming Hydrogen, increasing the pressure) 
Must be kept up to a height of 160 cm. Special requirements as to safety notices apply to the storage of large quantities, 
see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 518, 1994.

Colourless liquid, molecular weight 74.08 g/mol, density 0.99 g/cm3, water solubility 1000 g/L, soluble in ethanol, 
diethyl ether and chloroform, pH 2.5 (10g/L, 20C), melting point -22C, boiling point 141-142C, flash point 51C, vapour

Spreads in the aquatic environment.

Considered to be readily biodegradable.

Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.

State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if not possible, use a respiratory protective device with 
gas filter E and a particle filter. The choice of filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4.). 
Filtering respiratory protective device must only be used 3 hours a day. 
Use safety goggles. 
Use protective face shield and e.g. apron. 
It is not possible to find a suitable disposable glove according to the Quick Selection Guide to Chemical Protective 
Clothing (Fifth Edition). It is recommended to use disposal nitrile rubber gloves. The penetration time is not known, but 
is expected to be short. It is recommended that the gloves are to be renewed immediately in case of any spillage. Wash 
your hands thoroughly with soap and water before using new gloves. 
 
==PAGE:132==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible.

electricity. Ensure all equipment is earthed. 
Arrangements must be made to avoid contact with the substance. 
Frequency of use 
weekly 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
Evacuate the area as necessary. Switch off all apparatus. Prevent further spreading and ventilate the area.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Soak up any spillages with vermiculite or sand and discard as chemical waste.

occur.

water or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, 
taking along these instructions. 
In case of burns: rinse immediately with water and continue until pain disappears, while rinsing with water, remove 
clothing which is not burnt on the skin, go to emergency department at nearest hospital and continue to rinse with water 
en route.

This compound is published at the list of harmonized classification. 
Colourless or pale yellow liquid, molecular weight 102.15 g/mol, density 0.938 g/cm3, water solubility 40 g/L, very 
soluble in ethanol, diethyl ether and carbon tetrachloride, pH 2.7, melting point -34.5C, boiling point 186-187C, flash 
point 86C, vapour pressure 0.196 mmHg, autoignition temperature 375C, explosive limit 1.6-7.6 % v/v.Incompatible 
with a.o. strong oxidizing agents. bases, reducing agents, amines, nitriles, isocyanates. Copper nickel, aluminium, zinc 
(generation of hydrogen). The substance can form explosive mixtures with atmospheric air. 
Spreads in the aquatic environment. Is potentially biodegradable. Not considered to be bio-accumulating. Harmful to 
aquatic organisms, may cause long-term adverse effects in the aquatic environment. 
Description of safe work process  
Necessary precautions and safety arrangements (SDS, section C) 
Evaluate every unit operation: Weighing, heating, use of eluents, tidying up, etc.  
State approx. concentrations and amounts, etc. Attach the complete protocol if necessary.  
State needs for ventilation, gloves, glasses and other personal protections or special first aid equipment. 
Ensure effective process ventilation e.g. fume cupboards, and if not possible, use a respiratory protective device with 
combination filter ABEK and a particle filter. The choice of filter depends of the work (see Guide D.5.4.). 
Filtering respiratory protective device must only be used 3 hours a day. 
Use safety goggles. 
Use protective face shield and e.g. apron. 
It is not possible to find a suitable disposable glove according to the Quick Selection Guide to Chemical Protective 
Clothing (Fifth Edition). It is recommended to use disposal nitrile rubber gloves. The penetration time is not known, but 
is expected to be short. It is recommended that the gloves are to be renewed immediately in case of any spillage. Wash 
your hands thoroughly with soap and water before using new gloves. 
Emergency showers, eye wash bottles and sink with soap must be easily accessible. 
Flammable substance: never expose or store the substance near open flame. 
The substance has a low vapour pressure and will only emit limited vapour. NB: Heating, spraying or other aerosol-
generating work processes considerably increase the risk of inhalation. 
Frequency of use 
weekly 
Precautions regarding accidental spill and waste handling  (SDS, section F) 
==PAGE:137==
Chemical Risk Assessment at DTU Chemical Engineering 
AMU approved, 22-06-2016 
E.g. actions in case of accidents, collection and handling of spill. Remember waste handling of mixed products. 
Evacuate the area as necessary. Switch off all apparatus. Prevent further spreading and ventilate the area.  
Avoid contact with the substance. If appropriate, use gloves and breathing equipment with a combination filter (Type 
ABEK-P).  
Soak up any spillages with vermiculite or sand and discard as chemical waste.  
Clean the area after clearing up the spillage.  
Inform the municipal authorities and those responsible for the environment in the workplace of any major discharge into 
the surroundings. 
In case of fire, hazardous decomposition products are formed, e.g. carbon monoxide. Extinguishing by carbon dioxide 
or dry chemical powder. Be aware that dry chemical powder may damage your apparatus. 
In case of inhalation: The person should be taken into the fresh air, kept quiet and under supervision, if there is a risk of 
loss of consciousness the person should be placed in recovery position and kept warm, if person stops breathing, 
artificial respiration should be given. 
In case of skin contact: rinse thoroughly with water, remove contaminated clothing and any jewellery. 
In case of eye contact: rinse immediately with water, hold the eye wide open, remove contact lenses, continue to rinse 
with water until treatment is taken over by doctors. 
If swallowed: immediately rinse the mouth out and drink water or milk, do not give liquids to the unconscious, do not 
cause vomiting, if discomfort persists consult a doctor, taking along these instructions. 
In case of burns: rinse immediately with water and continue until pain disappears, while rinsing with water remove 
clothing which is not burnt on the skin, go to emergency department at nearest hospital and continue to rinse with water 
en route. 
Collection of waste  (SDS, section F. Evt attach yellow cancer  warning) 
Waste group 
UN no. 
Chemical Waste, Class 8, Class. Code C3, PGII 
C 
3265 
Safety instructions for storage  (SDS, section H) 
Original chemicals, stock solutions and products: E.g. sign posting, ventilation, cooling 
Must be stored tightly sealed in a well-ventilated chemical cabinet. 
Avoid sources of ignition (heat, electrical installations). Special requirements for safety notices apply to the storage of 
large quantities, see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 
518, 1994 and Danish Emergency Management Agency Authority Statutory order on flammable and combustible liquids 
No. 1639 of 06/12/2016 and associated guidance from 19 January 2017. 
 
Flammable liquid, class 
III-1. 
There must be stored up to 50 storage units per laboratory or chemical storage, or up to 800 units per fire section. (1 
storage unit corresponds to 50 liter). See Danish Emergency Management Agency Authority Statutory order on 
flammable and combustible liquids No. 1639 of 06/12/2016 and associated guidance from 19 January 2017. 
Must be kept up to a height of 160 cm. Special requirements as to safety notices apply to the storage of large quantities, 
see the Danish Working Environment Authority Statutory Order on safety notices as amended by no. 518, 1994. 
Substitution analysis  (SDS, section I)

2 Introduction 
The fermentation pilot plant is a semi-industrial installation of British origin, but refurbished by the 
department with modern instrumentation, control board and computer operation. It is installed in 
room 037 in building 228. Neither the building nor the plant is designed for GMO’s or 
toxic/pathogenic microorganisms. The plant is operated under the bio security law and for this 
reason under special application control. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:162==
Page 3 of 16 
 
3 Plant description 
The plant consists of the following units: 
 A mainframe (I) including a 70 liter stainless steel fermenter. 
 A mainframe (II) including a 20 liter glass fermenter. (can also be used with an optional 5 
liter fermenter) 
 A control board. 
 A computer with LabVIEW software for operation. 
 A utility and supply construction with space for 3 vessels on weighing cells used for 
nutrients, acid/base etc. In addition there is access to process steam, cooling water, 
compressed air and nitrogen. 
 A mobile heating unit which can be connected to either fermenter frame. 
 A mobile cooling unit which can be connected to either fermenter frame. 
The plant is operated from the computer, from where there is access to control valves, pumps, 
stirrers etc. Process parameters such as temperature, pH, oxygen etc. can be read and logged by the 
computer program LabVIEW (See section 6). Cooling water, process steam and compressed air is 
coming from the central supply systems and main valves and flow adjustments must be performed 
manually (See section 4). Nitrogen is supplied from an external bottle and must also be connected 
and controlled manually. Both heating and chilling water for the fermentation is controlled by 
separate mobile supply units. Figure 1 is a photo of the two fermenters, their mainframe and 
operational computer.  
 
==PAGE:163==
Page 4 of 16 
 
 
 
The PI-diagram for the fermenter pilot plant is schemed in Figure 2. The instruments regarding 
utility supplies are indicated with a single number digit whereas the instruments for the fermenters 
are with three digit numbers. The instruments starting with 1xx are for the left 70 liter fermenter and 
2xx for the small fermenter to the right. Valves illustrated with 
 are controlled in LabVIEW, 
while all other valves are manually controlled.  
 
Figure 1 - Photo of the fermentor pilot plant showing the two fermentors, mainframe and the operational computer.

Figure 2 - PI-diagram of the fermenter pilot plant. 
Each of the mainframes can be operated individually, but only one at a time according to the 
procedures in the LabVIEW software. The vessels T1, T2, T3 and T4 as well as the utilities are 
used for both mainframes, but controlled from the computer. 
 
Peristaltic pumps: 
P101, P102, P201 and P202 can run in manual or automatic mode. In manual mode they are 
controlled by a signal from 0 to 100 % of the pumps capacity. The system always works with both 
peristaltic pumps; the control “Acid/Base” button indicates for the user which one is effective in 
manual mode. In automatic mode the two pumps are controlled by the pH set point ranging from 0 
to 14. The control is a PID loop with default PID parameters optimized for short-term fermentation 
experiments (P=10; I=100; D=0).  P101/P201 controls the addition of acid and P102/P202 controls 
the addition of base. 
P103 and P203 are for adding feed or nutrients. There are three ways to operate these pumps. 
==PAGE:165==
Page 6 of 16 
 
Manual: 
In manual mode P103 or P203 are controlled by a signal from 0 to 100 % of the pump capacity. It is 
defined as time running per total time; pump speed is constant. In order to use automatic mode, 
manual mode has to be set to 0% pump capacity. 
pH dependent control: 
If pH dependent flow is desired, automatic mode should be activated. For the function to work a 
desired feed flow, 0 % pump capacity should be inserted when in manually mode in LabVIEW. The 
mode is then switched to automatic. The user set a set point for the pH between 0 and 14. If the 
measure pH is below the set point, base will be added; if pH is higher than the set point, acid is 
added. The loop is PID value controlled.  
Oxygen dependent control: 
If oxygen dependent feed flow is desired, automatic mode should be activated. For the function to 
work a desired feed flow, 0 % pump capacity, should be inserted when in manually mode in 
LabVIEW, see Figure 5. The mode is then switched to automatic. The user then has to determine a 
set point for the oxygen level. If the oxygen level is below the set point the pump will be inactive. If 
the oxygen level becomes greater than the set point the pump will be activated with the feed flow 
determined in the manual mode. As long the oxygen level is above the set point the pump will run 
with this determined flow.  
Exponential feed: 
The exponential feed can be turned on when the Manual/Automatic O2 is set to manual, see Figure 
5. To start the exponential feed the box below (Exponential) is set to automatic. Before starting the 
exponential feed all input variables needs to be given in a separate LabVIEW program, Figure 7. 
The exponential feed is controlled by the following equation.  
𝐹(𝑡) = 𝑌𝑥𝑠⋅𝜇0
𝑐𝑠,𝑓
⋅𝑋0 ⋅𝑉0 ⋅exp(𝜇0 ⋅𝑡) 
The units of the input variables are indicated in the LabVIEW control screen. For more information 
about operating in LabVIEW see Section 6. 
Stirrers: 
The stirrer is started and stopped from LabVIEW. The motor speed is controlled by a signal from 0 
to 100 % motor capacity and the RPM R value can be read of from LabVIEW.  
Cooling water: 
==PAGE:166==
Page 7 of 16 
 
The main valves are located on the wall behind the fermenter pilot plant and controlled manually. 
The inlet and outlet magnet valves V103, V203, V106 and V206 are opened/closed from 
LabVIEW. 
Process steam/condensate: 
The main valves are located on the wall behind the fermenter pilot plant and controlled manually. 
The steam and condensate valves V101, V201, V105 and V205 are opened/closed from LabVIEW. 
Remember that process steam and cooling water cannot be used at the same time and the one 
set of valves must be closed manually before the other set is opened. 
Compressed air: 
The flow is adjusted manually using the valves V111 and V211 on the mainframes. Rotameters 
FI101 and FI201 on the mainframes are recording the flow. FT102 and FT202 are electronic mass 
flow recorders with reading presented in LabVIEW. 
Nitrogen: 
The flow is adjusted manually using the valves V113 and V213 on the mainframe and record the 
flow on the rotameters FI103 and FI203. 
Weighing cells: 
The vessel T1, T2 and T3 are placed on weighing cells – WT1, WT2 and WT3 – and the readings 
are seen from LabVIEW. 
3.1 Component specification 
Oxygen electrode AIC102/202: One electrode for the two units. Reads % saturation (0-300 %). 
pH electrode AIC101/201: One electrode for the two units. 
Temperature sensors TIC101/201: Pt 100, located in the fermenters 
Manometers PI101/201: - 0.6 to 2 barg.  Manometer PI102 (jacket):  0 to 4 barg. 
Vessel working pressure: 2 barg, Jacket working pressure: 4 barg.  
Compressed air pressure: 3 bar. 
Compressed nitrogen pressure: variable through central back wall connection 
Rotameters: FI101: 50 l/min. FI103: 5000 cm3/min. FI201: 25 l/min. FI203: 5000 cm3/min. 
Pump capacity: P101/P102/P103 has a maximum speed of 660 mL/h. 
 
==PAGE:167==
Page 8 of 16 
 
4 Operation procedures 
4.1 Assembly 
Preparations: 
The position of V112 and V212 must not be changed (button arrow horizontal to the right). Flow of 
air or nitrogen is controlled by V111/V211 resp. V113/V213. 
Assembly: 
The fermenter should be standing attached to the mainframe, disassembled and clean. The assembly 
can vary depending on the experiment to be performed. This is a basic assembly of all necessary 
steps.  
 Dislocate the fermenter from the mainframe and transport it safely to the crane next to the 
setup. Attach the robe to the lid of the fermenter at the handles and lift the top off the vessel. 
 Baffles, impellers, dip pipe and aeration pipe can now be attached to the vessel and bottom 
of the fermenter lid. 
 The fermenter lid is attached properly into the tip at the bottom (look through a hole in the 
lid with a flash light) and sealed with screws.  
 Lid plugs and ports (including condenser, manometer and safety valve) are placed according 
to the description on the fermenter lid. Remember to control the O-rings and blue 
membranes. Control if the valve and plug in the bottom of the fermenter is closed. 
 Transfer the fermenter to the mainframe and attach it with the two screws – make sure that 
the fermenter is placed correctly by attaching the rotor from the mainframe to the fermenter. 
 Probes (pH and DO) are placed in the holes on the side of the fermenter. Side plugs block 
the rest. Remember that the pH probe should not dry out (use of appropriate storage 
solution). 
 
Connections: 
The assembled fermenter needs to be connected with the computer and other utilities to operate. 
 Probes are connected with their respective connectors. Temperature probe goes into the 
mainframe whereas for pH (AIC101) and DO (AIC102) separate connectors are below the 
mainframe.  
 The in- and outlet of the integrated and combined process steam, cooling water and 
condensate steel hoses are connected to the fermenter jacket. 
==PAGE:168==
Page 9 of 16 
 
 The tube from V120/V210 is connected to a vacuum flask and further connected to the gas 
outlet above the fermenter.  
 At the gas outlet the carbon dioxide probe is attached to the upper hole, see Figure 3. 
 
Figure 3 - Photograph of gas outlet including the orange CO2 probe attached 
 Connect the cooling unit (5.2) to the condenser on the vessels lid. 
 When required the air/nitrogen hose from V112/V212 is connected to the air inlet fermenter 
lid. 
 
After sterilisation: 
 Hoses from T1, T2 and T3 are connected to the pumps P101/P201, P102/P202 and 
P103/P203. The tips are connected to their respective inlets in the fermenter lid by pressing 
it through the blue membrane. 
 Sample outlet is pressed through the blue membrane in the lid of the fermenter marked as 
dip pipe. 
 The external temperature control unit is replacing the integrated combined steam/cooling 
pipes. See section 5.1 for more details about the external temperature control unit. 
4.2 Pressure test 
After assembling of the fermenter a pressure test should be performed to ensure the fermenter is 
tight and assembled correct. The pressure test is carried out by applying air to the fermenter. Air is 
blown into the fermenter until a pressure of approximately 1.2 bar. When the correct pressure is 
attained, the fermenter is observed whether it is capable to sustain the pressure. If the fermenter is 
==PAGE:169==
Page 10 of 16 
 
capable of sustaining the pressure, further air is added to the fermenter until a pressure of 
approximately 1.5 barg is obtained, to check if the relief valve sets off.  
The same result can be obtained by measuring inlet and outlet gas flow and using soup solution to 
detect unsealed connections. 
4.3 Sterilisation 
Sterilisation of microbial inactivation is performed after each fermentation. For every new setup 
(every new assembling) a pressure test has to be completed. 
The system is first drained of water (ensure steam piping pointing to save direction, open main 
steam valve, open blue safety valve, open steam inlet V201 and concensate outlet V205 in 
LabVIEW). When the system is drained, all valves are closed, water supply is turned off. 
 Steam/condensate piping is connected to the fermenter jacket. Mixing of the fermenter is then 
initiated to approximately 100 rpm to distribute the heat; condenser tower cooling is turned on. The 
steam supply and return valves should be opened manually on the wall behind the fermenter to 
ensure a pressure of approximately 2.0 barg on PI2. Next steam inlet V101/V201 and condensate 
outlet V105/V205 valves are opened in LabVIEW. A pressure of 1.5 barg should be obtained on the 
fermenter jacket (PI102). The sterilisation should run for 45 minutes with a temperate at 121°C and 
an internal pressure of 1.2 barg. For inactivation, a temperature of 95-97°C is necessary. Remember 
to bleed a little bit of air through the bottom of the filter casing (V121/V221) to ensure that the air is 
saturated with steam and to empty the case for condensed water. 
After 45 minutes sterilisation the steam supply valve and V101/V201 and V105/V205 are closed in 
LabVIEW. The fermenter jacket valve is carefully opened to empty the remaining steam. Next 
cooling water is added to the jacket by opening the cooling water supply and return valves 
V05/V06/V07 manually and the water outlet V106/V206 (has to be opened first) and inlet 
V103/V203 in LabVIEW. Cooling of the fermenter will result in a loss of pressure and create an 
undesired vacuum in the fermenter. This is resolved by lowering the pressure to approximately 0.1 
barg by opening V120/V220 at approximately 100°C. Compressed air is then coupled to the vessel 
and air is applied by V111/V211 to ensure ambient pressure inside the vessel. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:170==
Page 11 of 16 
 
4.4 Shutdown  
When shutting down the fermenter, it has to be sterilised and neutralised before it can be emptied. 
The sterilisation is carried out following the sterilisation procedure. The neutralisation is done by 
adding either acid or base until a pH between 6.0 to 8.0 is reached. 
To empty the 70L fermenter, a tube is connected to the bottom valve on the fermenter and the 
drainage valve is opened. Compressed air can be added to pressurise the vessel to increase the flow 
of broth out of the fermenter while stirring. If the media contains high amounts of heavy metals the 
broth should be collected in cans, otherwise it can just be drained. 
The fermenter is then disassembled and all detachable parts are removed and washed. All parts and 
the fermenter are washed using water, brushes and sponges without the use of soap! 
5 External operating units 
5.1 External mobile heating unit (TEMPRO primus C90) 
The mobile heating unit is used to sustain moderate temperatures in the fermenter (20-40°C). The 
following step-by-step guide describes the usage.  
 The heating unit is connected to the fermenters jacket by replacing steam/cooling couplings.  
 The water valve to the Primus is partially opened. Full opening causes the internal valve to 
block and prevents the unit from cooling; fermenter is overheated in following operation.  
 The Primus waste water tube is placed in drainage.  
 The Primus is turned on. 
 The set point for the required temperature, SET1, is determined on the Primus display (in 
°C). 
 The set point for the built-in cooling function, SET2, is determined on the Primus display, if 
cooling is required (in °C). 
 The tolerance for temperature fluctuations, TOL, is defined (in °C). 
The mobile heating unit will obtain the defined temperature in SET1. If the cooling button is 
pressed, a yellow light will indicate that cooling has begun and set point SET2 will be obtained.  
5.2 External mobile cooling unit  
The mobile cooling unit is used to cool the condenser. The following step-by-step guide describes 
the usage. 
==PAGE:171==
Page 12 of 16 
 
 The volume of the water bath is examined whether if additional water has to be filled in. 
 Tubing from the cooling unit’s stirring unit is connected to the condenser 
 Plug in power-cable. 
 Adjust the temperature of the water bath to the desired temperature. (OBS! Do not adjust 
the set point below the freezing point of the used liquid!) 
The mobile cooling unit flow will start immediately when power-cable is connected and is turned 
off by unplugging the power-cable.   
 
6 LabVIEW program 
In order to reach the LabVIEW control program the main switch on the left side of the control board 
as well as the two main switches on the top left of the two mainframes must be switched on. Then 
start the computer – if asked for password type in “blank”. Open the LabVIEW program by clicking 
the “start page shortcut”- icon on the screen. If exponential feed is to be used open 
“Exponential.vi”-icon as well. 
There are four important application screens: 
 Data file screen (Figure 4). Here you start and stop data logging, electronic read sensor 
outputs, change sampling rate, add comments etc. 
 Fermenter PI diagram screen (Figure 5). There are three folders: 
o Fermenter left. This is for operating the mainframe system with the large 70 liter 
fermenter. Operating data are read, set points defined and components activated. 
o Fermenter right. This is for the mainframe system with the 20 liter glass fermenter, 
and with the same kind of information and activities as above. 
o Maintenance (no figure). On this screen you can change PID parameters for the pH 
controllers and change pumping direction for the acid/base and feed pumps. This 
folder is only for super users. 
 Graph screen (Figure 6). Selected data are continuously printed. 
 Exponential feed screen (Figure 7). Values for the exponential feed are submitted into this 
screen.

7 Maintenance 
7.1 Calibration of pH electrode 
The pH is measured by the Mettler Toledo instrument inside the mainframe in the cabinet. The pH 
is calibrated directly on this unit, by choosing the calibration mode on the mini display. One of the 
options is a two-step calibration, preferable with pH at 4.0 and 7.0. The day of calibration should be 
noted in the logbook.
